<p><b>Ashoka</b> (<span class="rt-commentedText nowrap"><span class="IPA nopopups noexcerpt" lang="en-fonipa"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/English" title="Help:IPA/English">/<span style="border-bottom:1px dotted"><span title="/ə/: 'a' in 'about'">ə</span><span title="/ˈ/: primary stress follows">ˈ</span><span title="/ʃ/: 'sh' in 'shy'">ʃ</span><span title="/oʊ/: 'o' in 'code'">oʊ</span><span title="'k' in 'kind'">k</span><span title="/ə/: 'a' in 'about'">ə</span></span>/</a></span></span>) <i>Asoka</i>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-6"><a href="#cite_note-6">[5]</a></sup> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/IAST" title="IAST">IAST</a>: <i><span title="International Alphabet of Sanskrit transliteration"><i lang="sa-Latn">Aśoka</i></span></i>; <abbr title="circa">c.</abbr> 304 – 232 BCE), popularly known as <b>Ashoka the Great</b>, was an <a href="/wiki/List_of_Indian_monarchs" title="List of Indian monarchs">Indian emperor</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Maurya_Empire" title="Maurya Empire">Maurya Empire</a>, son of <a href="/wiki/Bindusara" title="Bindusara">Bindusara</a>, who ruled almost the entire <a href="/wiki/Indian_subcontinent" title="Indian subcontinent">Indian subcontinent</a> from <abbr title="circa">c.</abbr><span style="white-space:nowrap;"> 268</span> to 232 BCE.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-7"><a href="#cite_note-7">[6]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEThapar198051_8-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEThapar198051-8">[7]</a></sup> Ashoka promoted the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Spread_of_Buddhism" title="Spread of Buddhism">spread of Buddhism</a> across ancient Asia.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Strong_2003_5-1"><a href="#cite_note-Strong_2003-5">[4]</a></sup> Considered by many to be one of India's greatest emperors, Ashoka expanded <a href="/wiki/Chandragupta_Maurya" title="Chandragupta Maurya">Chandragupta's</a> empire to reign over territory stretching from present-day <a href="/wiki/Afghanistan" title="Afghanistan">Afghanistan</a> in the west to present-day <a href="/wiki/Bangladesh" title="Bangladesh">Bangladesh</a> in the east. It covered the entire <a href="/wiki/Indian_subcontinent" title="Indian subcontinent">Indian subcontinent</a> except for parts of present-day <a href="/wiki/Kerala" title="Kerala">Kerala</a> and <a href="/wiki/Tamil_Nadu" title="Tamil Nadu">Tamil Nadu</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-9"><a href="#cite_note-9">[8]</a></sup> The empire's capital was <a href="/wiki/Pataliputra" title="Pataliputra">Pataliputra</a> (in <a href="/wiki/Magadha" title="Magadha">Magadha</a>, present-day <a href="/wiki/Patna" title="Patna">Patna</a>), with provincial capitals at <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Takshashila" title="Takshashila">Takshashila</a> (later <a href="/wiki/Taxila" title="Taxila">Taxila</a>) and <a href="/wiki/Ujjain" title="Ujjain">Ujjain</a>. Ashoka, after the <a href="/wiki/Kalinga_War" title="Kalinga War">war of Kalinga</a>, was upset with the bloodshed and vowed to never again wage a war of conquest. He patronised Buddhism during his reign.
</p><p>Ashoka waged a particularly destructive war against the state of <a href="/wiki/Kalinga_(historical_region)" title="Kalinga (historical region)">Kalinga</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBentley199344_10-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBentley199344-10">[9]</a></sup> which he conquered in about 260 BCE.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-11"><a href="#cite_note-11">[10]</a></sup> According to an interpretation of <a href="/wiki/Edicts_of_Ashoka" title="Edicts of Ashoka">his Edicts</a>, he converted to <a href="/wiki/Buddhism" title="Buddhism">Buddhism</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBentley199344_10-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBentley199344-10">[9]</a></sup> after witnessing the mass deaths of the <a href="/wiki/Kalinga_War" title="Kalinga War">Kalinga War</a>, which he had waged out of a desire for conquest and which reportedly directly resulted in more than 100,000 deaths and 150,000 deportations.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBentley199345_12-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBentley199345-12">[11]</a></sup> He is remembered for erecting the <a href="/wiki/Pillars_of_Ashoka" title="Pillars of Ashoka">Ashoka pillars</a> and spreading his Edicts,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-HR_13-0"><a href="#cite_note-HR-13">[12]</a></sup> for sending <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Buddhist_monks" title="Buddhist monks">Buddhist monks</a> to <a href="/wiki/Sri_Lanka" title="Sri Lanka">Sri Lanka</a> and <a href="/wiki/Central_Asia" title="Central Asia">Central Asia</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Strong_2003_5-2"><a href="#cite_note-Strong_2003-5">[4]</a></sup> and for establishing monuments marking several significant sites in the life of <a href="/wiki/Gautama_Buddha" title="Gautama Buddha">Gautama Buddha</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBentley199346_14-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBentley199346-14">[13]</a></sup>
</p><p>Beyond the <a href="/wiki/Edicts_of_Ashoka" title="Edicts of Ashoka">Edicts of Ashoka</a>, biographical information about him relies on legends written centuries later, such as the 2nd-century CE <i><a href="/wiki/Ashokavadana" title="Ashokavadana">Ashokavadana</a></i> ("<i>Narrative of Ashoka</i>", a part of the <i><a href="/wiki/Divyavadana" title="Divyavadana">Divyavadana</a></i>), and in the Sri Lankan text <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mahavamsa" title="Mahavamsa">Mahavamsa</a></i> ("<i>Great Chronicle</i>"). The emblem of the modern <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Republic_of_India" title="Republic of India">Republic of India</a> is an adaptation of the <a href="/wiki/Lion_Capital_of_Ashoka" title="Lion Capital of Ashoka">Lion Capital of Ashoka</a>. His <a href="/wiki/Sanskrit" title="Sanskrit">Sanskrit</a> name "<span title="International Alphabet of Sanskrit transliteration"><i lang="sa-Latn">Aśoka</i></span>" means "painless, without sorrow" (the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/A_privativum" title="A privativum"><i>a</i> privativum</a> and <i>śoka</i>, "pain, distress"). In his <a href="/wiki/Edicts_of_Ashoka" title="Edicts of Ashoka">edicts</a>, he is referred to as <i><span title="International Alphabet of Sanskrit transliteration"><i lang="sa-Latn">Devānāmpriya</i></span></i> (Pali <i><span title="International Alphabet of Sanskrit transliteration"><i lang="sa-Latn">Devānaṃpiya</i></span></i> or "the Beloved of the Gods"), and <i><span title="International Alphabet of Sanskrit transliteration"><i lang="sa-Latn">Priyadarśin</i></span></i> or Priyadarshi (Pali <i><span title="International Alphabet of Sanskrit transliteration"><i lang="sa-Latn"><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Piyadas%C4%AB" title="Piyadasī">Piyadasī</a></i></span></i> or "He who regards everyone with affection"). His fondness for a tree is the reason for his name being connected to the "Ashoka tree" or <i><a href="/wiki/Saraca_asoca" title="Saraca asoca">Saraca asoca</a></i>, and this is referenced in the <i><a href="/wiki/Ashokavadana" title="Ashokavadana">Ashokavadana</a></i>.
</p><p>In <i><a href="/wiki/The_Outline_of_History" title="The Outline of History">The Outline of History</a></i> (1920), <a href="/wiki/H._G._Wells" title="H. G. Wells">H. G. Wells</a> wrote, "Amidst the tens of thousands of names of monarchs that crowd the columns of history, their majesties and graciousnesses and serenities and royal highnesses and the like, the name of Ashoka shines, and shines, almost alone, a star."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lahiri2015_15-0"><a href="#cite_note-Lahiri2015-15">[14]</a></sup>
</p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Sources_of_information">Sources of information</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ashoka&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Sources of information">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Information about Ashoka comes from <a href="/wiki/List_of_Edicts_of_Ashoka" title="List of Edicts of Ashoka">his inscriptions</a>; other inscriptions that mention him or are possibly from his reign; and ancient literature, especially Buddhist texts.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEThapar19615–8_16-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEThapar19615–8-16">[15]</a></sup> These sources often contradict each other, although various historians have attempted to correlate their testimony.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESingh2012132_17-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESingh2012132-17">[16]</a></sup> Plenty is known or not known. So, for example, while Ashoka is often attributed with building many hospitals during his time, there is no clear evidence that any hospitals existed in ancient India during the 3rd century BC or that Ashoka was responsible for commissioning the construction of any.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-18"><a href="#cite_note-18">[17]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Ashoka_Rock_Edict_at_Junagadh.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="960" data-file-width="1280" decoding="async" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/40/Ashoka_Rock_Edict_at_Junagadh.jpg/220px-Ashoka_Rock_Edict_at_Junagadh.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/40/Ashoka_Rock_Edict_at_Junagadh.jpg/330px-Ashoka_Rock_Edict_at_Junagadh.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/40/Ashoka_Rock_Edict_at_Junagadh.jpg/440px-Ashoka_Rock_Edict_at_Junagadh.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Ashoka_Rock_Edict_at_Junagadh.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ashoka%27s_Major_Rock_Edict" title="Ashoka's Major Rock Edict">Ashoka's Major Rock Edict</a> at <a href="/wiki/Junagadh" title="Junagadh">Junagadh</a> contains inscriptions by Ashoka (fourteen of the <a href="/wiki/Edicts_of_Ashoka" title="Edicts of Ashoka">Edicts of Ashoka</a>), <a href="/wiki/Rudradaman_I" title="Rudradaman I">Rudradaman I</a> and <a href="/wiki/Skandagupta" title="Skandagupta">Skandagupta</a>.</div></div></div>
<p><b>Inscriptions</b>
</p><p>Ashoka's inscriptions<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-19"><a href="#cite_note-19">[18]</a></sup> are the earliest self-representations of imperial power in the Indian subcontinent.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESingh2012131_20-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESingh2012131-20">[19]</a></sup> However, these inscriptions are focused mainly on the topic of <i><a href="/wiki/Ashoka%27s_policy_of_Dhamma" title="Ashoka's policy of Dhamma">dhamma</a></i>, and provide little information regarding other aspects of the Maurya state and society.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESingh2012132_17-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESingh2012132-17">[16]</a></sup> Even on the topic of <i>dhamma</i>, the content of these inscriptions cannot be taken at face value. In the words of American academic <a href="/wiki/John_S._Strong" title="John S. Strong">John S. Strong</a>, it is sometimes helpful to think of Ashoka's messages as propaganda by a politician whose aim is to present a favourable image of himself and his administration, rather than record historical facts. <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEStrong1995141_21-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEStrong1995141-21">[20]</a></sup>
</p><p>A small number of other inscriptions also provide some information about Ashoka.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESingh2012132_17-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESingh2012132-17">[16]</a></sup> For example, he finds a mention in the 2nd century <a href="/wiki/Junagadh_rock_inscription_of_Rudradaman" title="Junagadh rock inscription of Rudradaman">Junagadh rock inscription of Rudradaman</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEThapar19618_22-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEThapar19618-22">[21]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Aramaic_Inscription_of_Taxila" title="Aramaic Inscription of Taxila">An inscription</a> discovered at <a href="/wiki/Sirkap" title="Sirkap">Sirkap</a> mentions a lost word beginning with "Priy", which is theorised to be Ashoka's title "Priyadarshi", although this is not certain.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEThapar19617_23-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEThapar19617-23">[22]</a></sup> Some other inscriptions, such as the <a href="/wiki/Sohgaura_copper_plate_inscription" title="Sohgaura copper plate inscription">Sohgaura copper plate inscription</a>, have been tentatively dated to Ashoka's period by a section of scholars, although others contest this. <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEThapar19617–8_24-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEThapar19617–8-24">[23]</a></sup>
</p><p><b>Buddhist legends</b>
</p><p>Much of the information about Ashoka comes from Buddhist legends, which present him as a great, ideal king.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESingh2008331–332_25-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESingh2008331–332-25">[24]</a></sup> These legends appear in texts that are not contemporary to Ashoka and were composed by Buddhist authors, who used various stories to illustrate the impact of their faith on Ashoka. This makes it necessary to exercise caution while relying on them for historical information.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEThapar19618–9_26-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEThapar19618–9-26">[25]</a></sup> Among modern scholars, opinions range from downright dismissal of these legends as mythological to acceptance of all historical portions that seem plausible.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEStrong198912_27-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEStrong198912-27">[26]</a></sup>
</p><p>The Buddhist legends about Ashoka exist in several languages, including <a href="/wiki/Sanskrit" title="Sanskrit">Sanskrit</a>, <a href="/wiki/Pali" title="Pali">Pali</a>, <a href="/wiki/Tibetic_languages" title="Tibetic languages">Tibetan</a>, <a href="/wiki/Chinese_language" title="Chinese language">Chinese</a>, <a href="/wiki/Burmese_language" title="Burmese language">Burmese</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sinhala_language" title="Sinhala language">Sinhala</a>, <a href="/wiki/Thai_language" title="Thai language">Thai</a>, <a href="/wiki/Lao_language" title="Lao language">Lao</a>, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Khotanese_language" title="Khotanese language">Khotanese</a>. All these legends can be traced to two primary traditions:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEStrong1995143_28-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEStrong1995143-28">[27]</a></sup>
</p>
<ul><li>the North Indian tradition preserved in the Sanskrit-language texts such as <i><a href="/wiki/Divyavadana" title="Divyavadana">Divyavadana</a></i> (including its constituent <i><a href="/wiki/Ashokavadana" title="Ashokavadana">Ashokavadana</a></i>); and Chinese sources such as <i>A-yü wang chuan</i> and <i>A-yü wang ching</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEStrong1995143_28-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEStrong1995143-28">[27]</a></sup></li>
<li>the Sri Lankan tradition preserved in Pali-lanuage texts, such as <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Dipavamsa" title="Dipavamsa">Dipavamsa</a></i>, <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mahavamsa" title="Mahavamsa">Mahavamsa</a></i>, <i>Vamsatthapakasini</i> (a commentary on <i>Mahavamsa</i>), Buddhaghosha's commentary on the Vinaya, and <i>Samanta-pasadika</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEStrong1995143_28-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEStrong1995143-28">[27]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEThapar19618_22-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEThapar19618-22">[21]</a></sup></li></ul>
<p>There are several significant differences between the two traditions. For example, the Sri Lankan tradition emphasizes Ashoka's role in convening the <a href="/wiki/Third_Buddhist_council" title="Third Buddhist council">Third Buddhist council</a>, and his dispatch of several missionaries to distant regions, including his son <a href="/wiki/Mahinda_(Buddhist_monk)" title="Mahinda (Buddhist monk)">Mahinda</a> to Sri Lanka.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEStrong1995143_28-3"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEStrong1995143-28">[27]</a></sup> However, the North Indian tradition makes no mention of these events. It describes other events not found in the Sri Lankan tradition, such as a story about another son named Kunala. <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEStrong1995144_29-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEStrong1995144-29">[28]</a></sup>
</p><p>Even while narrating the common stories, the two traditions diverge in several ways. For example, both <i>Ashokavadana</i> and <i>Mahavamsa</i> mention that Ashoka's queen <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tishyarakshita" title="Tishyarakshita">Tishyarakshita</a> had the <a href="/wiki/Bodhi_Tree" title="Bodhi Tree">Bodhi Tree</a> destroyed. In <i>Ashokavadana</i>, the queen manages to have the tree healed after she realises her mistake. In the <i>Mahavamsa</i>, she permanently destroys the tree, but only after a branch of the tree has been transplanted in Sri Lanka.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEStrong1995152–154_30-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEStrong1995152–154-30">[29]</a></sup> In another story, both the texts describe Ashoka's unsuccessful attempts to collect a relic of Gautama Buddha from <a href="/wiki/Ramagrama_stupa" title="Ramagrama stupa">Ramagrama</a>. In <i>Ashokavadana</i>, he fails to do so because he cannot match the devotion of the Nagas who hold the relic; however, in the <i>Mahavamsa</i>, he fails to do so because the Buddha had destined the relic to be enshrined by King <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Dutthagamani" title="Dutthagamani">Dutthagamani</a> of Sri Lanka. <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEStrong1995155_31-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEStrong1995155-31">[30]</a></sup> Using such stories, the <i>Mahavamsa</i> glorifies Sri Lanka as the new preserve of Buddhism. <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEStrong1995154–157_32-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEStrong1995154–157-32">[31]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="center"><div class="thumb tnone"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:802px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:013_King_Asoka_visits_Ramagrama_(33428090870).jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1484" data-file-width="5072" decoding="async" height="234" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/89/013_King_Asoka_visits_Ramagrama_%2833428090870%29.jpg/800px-013_King_Asoka_visits_Ramagrama_%2833428090870%29.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/89/013_King_Asoka_visits_Ramagrama_%2833428090870%29.jpg/1200px-013_King_Asoka_visits_Ramagrama_%2833428090870%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/89/013_King_Asoka_visits_Ramagrama_%2833428090870%29.jpg/1600px-013_King_Asoka_visits_Ramagrama_%2833428090870%29.jpg 2x" width="800"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:013_King_Asoka_visits_Ramagrama_(33428090870).jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>King Ashoka visits Ramagrama, to take relics of the Buddha from the <a href="/wiki/N%C4%81ga" title="Nāga">Nagas</a>, but in vain. Southern gateway, Stupa 1, <a href="/wiki/Sanchi" title="Sanchi">Sanchi</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESingh2017162_2-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESingh2017162-2">[2]</a></sup></div></div></div></div>
<p><b>Other sources</b>
</p><p>Numismatic, sculptural, and archaeological evidence supplements research on Ashoka.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEThapar196111_33-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEThapar196111-33">[32]</a></sup> Ashoka's name appears in the lists of Mauryan kings in the various <a href="/wiki/Puranas" title="Puranas">Puranas</a>. However, these texts do not provide further details about him, as their <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Brahmanism" title="Brahmanism">Brahmanical</a> authors were not patronised by the Mauryans.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEThapar199515_34-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEThapar199515-34">[33]</a></sup> Other texts, such as the <i><a href="/wiki/Arthashastra" title="Arthashastra">Arthashastra</a></i> and <i><a href="/wiki/Indica_(Megasthenes)" title="Indica (Megasthenes)">Indica of Megasthenes</a></i>, which provide general information about the Maurya period, can also be used to make inferences about Ashoka's reign.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEThapar19619_35-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEThapar19619-35">[34]</a></sup> However, the <i>Arthashastra</i> is a normative text that focuses on an ideal rather than a historical state, and its dating to the Mauryan period is a subject of debate. The <i>Indica</i> is a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Lost_work" title="Lost work">lost work</a>, and only parts of it survive in the form of paraphrases in later writings.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESingh2012132_17-3"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESingh2012132-17">[16]</a></sup>
</p><p>The 12th-century text <i><a href="/wiki/Rajatarangini" title="Rajatarangini">Rajatarangini</a></i> mentions a <a href="/wiki/Kashmiris" title="Kashmiris">Kashmiri</a> king <a href="/wiki/Ashoka_(Gonandiya)" title="Ashoka (Gonandiya)">Ashoka of Gonandiya dynasty</a> who built several <a href="/wiki/Stupa" title="Stupa">stupas</a>: some scholars, such as <a href="/wiki/Aurel_Stein" title="Aurel Stein">Aurel Stein</a>, have identified this king with the Maurya king Ashoka; others, such as <a href="/wiki/Ananda_W._P._Guruge" title="Ananda W. P. Guruge">Ananda W. P. Guruge</a> dismiss this identification as inaccurate.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGuruge,_Review1995185–188_36-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGuruge,_Review1995185–188-36">[35]</a></sup>
</p><p><b>Alternative interpretation of the epigraphic evidence</b>
</p>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1096954695/mw-parser-output/.tmulti">.mw-parser-output .tmulti .multiimageinner{display:flex;flex-direction:column}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .trow{display:flex;flex-direction:row;clear:left;flex-wrap:wrap;width:100%;box-sizing:border-box}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .tsingle{margin:1px;float:left}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .trow{justify-content:center}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .tsingle{float:none!important;max-width:100%!important;box-sizing:border-box;text-align:center}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .tsingle .thumbcaption{text-align:left}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .trow>.thumbcaption{text-align:center}}</style><div class="thumb tmulti tright"><div class="thumbinner multiimageinner" style="width:442px;max-width:442px"><div class="trow"><div class="theader">The Edicts and their declared authors</div></div><div class="trow"><div class="tsingle" style="width:219px;max-width:219px"><div class="thumbimage" style="height:200px;overflow:hidden"><a class="image" //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ac/Major_Rock_Edicts_and_Major_Pillar_Edicts_by_Devanampiya_Piyadasi.jpg/434px-Major_Rock_Edicts_and_Major_Pillar_Edicts_by_Devanampiya_Piyadasi.jpg 2x" width="217"/></a></div><div class="thumbcaption text-align-center">Edicts in the name of <a href="/wiki/Priyadasi" title="Priyadasi">Piyadasi</a> or <i><a class="mw-redirect" data-file-width="64" decoding="async" height="8" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e4/Brown_pog.svg/8px-Brown_pog.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e4/Brown_pog.svg/12px-Brown_pog.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e4/Brown_pog.svg/16px-Brown_pog.svg.png 2x" width="8"/></a>: <a href="/wiki/Major_Rock_Edicts" title="Major Rock Edicts">Major Rock Edicts</a><br/><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Brown_5C3317.svg"><img alt="Brown 5C3317.svg" data-file-height="1063" data-file-width="354" decoding="async" height="9" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/df/Brown_5C3317.svg/3px-Brown_5C3317.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/df/Brown_5C3317.svg/5px-Brown_5C3317.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/df/Brown_5C3317.svg/6px-Brown_5C3317.svg.png 2x" width="3"/></a>: <a href="/wiki/Major_Pillar_Edicts" title="Major Pillar Edicts">Major Pillar Edicts</a></div></div><div class="tsingle" style="width:219px;max-width:219px"><div class="thumbimage" style="height:200px;overflow:hidden"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Minor_Rock_Edicts_and_Minor_Pillar_Edicts_of_Ashoka.jpg"><img alt="" data-file-height="1668" data-file-width="1803" decoding="async" height="201" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2a/Minor_Rock_Edicts_and_Minor_Pillar_Edicts_of_Ashoka.jpg/217px-Minor_Rock_Edicts_and_Minor_Pillar_Edicts_of_Ashoka.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2a/Minor_Rock_Edicts_and_Minor_Pillar_Edicts_of_Ashoka.jpg/326px-Minor_Rock_Edicts_and_Minor_Pillar_Edicts_of_Ashoka.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2a/Minor_Rock_Edicts_and_Minor_Pillar_Edicts_of_Ashoka.jpg/434px-Minor_Rock_Edicts_and_Minor_Pillar_Edicts_of_Ashoka.jpg 2x" width="217"/></a></div><div class="thumbcaption text-align-center">Edicts in the name of Ashoka or just "<i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Devanampiya" title="Devanampiya">Devanampiya</a></i>" ("King"), or both together:<br/><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Orange_ff8040_pog.svg"><img alt="Orange ff8040 pog.svg" data-file-height="64" data-file-width="64" decoding="async" height="8" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/29/Orange_ff8040_pog.svg/8px-Orange_ff8040_pog.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/29/Orange_ff8040_pog.svg/12px-Orange_ff8040_pog.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/29/Orange_ff8040_pog.svg/16px-Orange_ff8040_pog.svg.png 2x" width="8"/></a>: <a href="/wiki/Minor_Rock_Edicts" title="Minor Rock Edicts">Minor Rock Edicts</a><br/><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Orange_F79A18.svg"><img alt="Orange F79A18.svg" data-file-height="1063" data-file-width="354" decoding="async" height="9" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/46/Orange_F79A18.svg/3px-Orange_F79A18.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/46/Orange_F79A18.svg/5px-Orange_F79A18.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/46/Orange_F79A18.svg/6px-Orange_F79A18.svg.png 2x" width="3"/></a>: <a href="/wiki/Minor_Pillar_Edicts" title="Minor Pillar Edicts">Minor Pillar Edicts</a></div></div></div><div class="trow" style="display:flow-root"><div class="thumbcaption" style="text-align:center">The different areas covered by the two types of inscriptions, and their different content in respect to Buddhism, may point to different rulers.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CIB226_37-0"><a href="#cite_note-CIB226-37">[36]</a></sup></div></div></div></div>
<p>For some scholars such as <a href="/wiki/Christopher_I._Beckwith" title="Christopher I. Beckwith">Christopher I. Beckwith</a>, Ashoka, whose name only appears in the <a href="/wiki/Minor_Rock_Edicts" title="Minor Rock Edicts">Minor Rock Edicts</a>, is not the same as king <a href="/wiki/Priyadasi" title="Priyadasi">Piyadasi</a>, or <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Devanampiya" title="Devanampiya">Devanampiya</a></i> Piyadasi (i.e. "Beloved of the Gods Piyadasi", "Beloved of the Gods" being a fairly widespread title for "King"), who is named as the author of the <a href="/wiki/Major_Pillar_Edicts" title="Major Pillar Edicts">Major Pillar Edicts</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Major_Rock_Edicts" title="Major Rock Edicts">Major Rock Edicts</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CIB226_37-1"><a href="#cite_note-CIB226-37">[36]</a></sup>
</p><p>Beckwith suggests that Piyadasi was living in the 3rd century BCE, was probably the son of <a href="/wiki/Chandragupta_Maurya" title="Chandragupta Maurya">Chandragupta Maurya</a> known to the Greeks as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Amitrochates" title="Amitrochates">Amitrochates</a>, and only advocated for piety ("<a href="/wiki/Dharma" title="Dharma">Dharma</a>") in his <a href="/wiki/Major_Pillar_Edicts" title="Major Pillar Edicts">Major Pillar Edicts</a> and <a href="/wiki/Major_Rock_Edicts" title="Major Rock Edicts">Major Rock Edicts</a>, without ever mentioning <a href="/wiki/Buddhism" title="Buddhism">Buddhism</a>, the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Buddha" title="Buddha">Buddha</a> or the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Samgha" title="Samgha">Samgha</a> (the single notable exception is the 7th Edict of the <a href="/wiki/Major_Pillar_Edicts" title="Major Pillar Edicts">Major Pillar Edicts</a> which does mention the Samgha, but is a considered a later fake by Beckwith).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CIB226_37-2"><a href="#cite_note-CIB226-37">[36]</a></sup> Also, the geographical spread of his inscription shows that Piyadasi ruled a vast Empire, contiguous with the <a href="/wiki/Seleucid_Empire" title="Seleucid Empire">Seleucid Empire</a> in the West.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CIB226_37-3"><a href="#cite_note-CIB226-37">[36]</a></sup>
</p><p>On the contrary, for Beckwith, Ashoka was <a href="/wiki/Ashoka_(Gonandiya)" title="Ashoka (Gonandiya)">a later king of the 1st–2nd century CE</a>, whose name only appears explicitly in the <a href="/wiki/Minor_Rock_Edicts" title="Minor Rock Edicts">Minor Rock Edicts</a> and allusively in the <a href="/wiki/Minor_Pillar_Edicts" title="Minor Pillar Edicts">Minor Pillar Edicts</a>, and who does mention the Buddha and the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Samgha" title="Samgha">Samgha</a>, explicitly promoting Buddhism.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CIB226_37-4"><a href="#cite_note-CIB226-37">[36]</a></sup> The name "Priyadarsi" does occur in two of the minor edicts (Gujarra and Bairat), but Beckwith again considers them as later fabrications.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CIB226_37-5"><a href="#cite_note-CIB226-37">[36]</a></sup> The minor inscriptions cover a very different and much smaller geographical area, clustering in Central India.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CIB226_37-6"><a href="#cite_note-CIB226-37">[36]</a></sup> According to Beckwith, the inscriptions of this later Ashoka were typical of the later forms of "normative Buddhism", which are well attested from inscriptions and Gandhari manuscripts dated to the turn of the millennium, and around the time of the <a href="/wiki/Kushan_Empire" title="Kushan Empire">Kushan Empire</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CIB226_37-7"><a href="#cite_note-CIB226-37">[36]</a></sup> The quality of the inscriptions of this Ashoka is significantly lower than the quality of the inscriptions of the earlier Piyadasi.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CIB226_37-8"><a href="#cite_note-CIB226-37">[36]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Names_and_titles">Names and titles</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ashoka&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Names and titles">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1096954695/mw-parser-output/.tmulti" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="thumb tmulti tright"><div class="thumbinner multiimageinner" style="width:292px;max-width:292px"><div class="trow"><div class="theader">Names and titles of Ashoka</div></div><div class="trow"><div class="tsingle" style="width:140px;max-width:140px"><div class="thumbimage" style="height:62px;overflow:hidden"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Devanampiyasa_Asoka.jpg"><img alt="" data-file-height="990" data-file-width="2208" decoding="async" height="62" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cd/Devanampiyasa_Asoka.jpg/138px-Devanampiyasa_Asoka.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cd/Devanampiyasa_Asoka.jpg/207px-Devanampiyasa_Asoka.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cd/Devanampiyasa_Asoka.jpg/276px-Devanampiyasa_Asoka.jpg 2x" width="138"/></a></div><div class="thumbcaption text-align-center">"<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Devanampiya" title="Devanampiya">Devānampiyasa</a> Asoka", in the <a href="/wiki/Maski" title="Maski">Maski</a> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Edict_of_Ashoka" title="Edict of Ashoka">Edict of Ashoka</a>.</div></div><div class="tsingle" style="width:148px;max-width:148px"><div class="thumbimage" style="height:62px;overflow:hidden"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Ashoka_in_Brahmi_script.jpg"><img alt="" data-file-height="234" data-file-width="549" decoding="async" height="62" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/37/Ashoka_in_Brahmi_script.jpg/146px-Ashoka_in_Brahmi_script.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/37/Ashoka_in_Brahmi_script.jpg/219px-Ashoka_in_Brahmi_script.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/37/Ashoka_in_Brahmi_script.jpg/292px-Ashoka_in_Brahmi_script.jpg 2x" width="146"/></a></div><div class="thumbcaption text-align-center">The name "Asoka" (𑀅𑀲𑁄𑀓 <i>A-so-ka</i>) in the <a href="/wiki/Maski" title="Maski">Maski</a> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Minor_Rock_Edict" title="Minor Rock Edict">Minor Rock Edict</a>.</div></div></div><div class="trow"><div class="tsingle" style="width:290px;max-width:290px"><div class="thumbimage" style="height:62px;overflow:hidden"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Devanampiyena_Piyadasi_in_the_Lumbini_Edict_of_Ashoka.jpg"><img alt="" data-file-height="348" data-file-width="1600" decoding="async" height="63" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1e/Devanampiyena_Piyadasi_in_the_Lumbini_Edict_of_Ashoka.jpg/288px-Devanampiyena_Piyadasi_in_the_Lumbini_Edict_of_Ashoka.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1e/Devanampiyena_Piyadasi_in_the_Lumbini_Edict_of_Ashoka.jpg/432px-Devanampiyena_Piyadasi_in_the_Lumbini_Edict_of_Ashoka.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1e/Devanampiyena_Piyadasi_in_the_Lumbini_Edict_of_Ashoka.jpg/576px-Devanampiyena_Piyadasi_in_the_Lumbini_Edict_of_Ashoka.jpg 2x" width="288"/></a></div><div class="thumbcaption text-align-center">Ashoka's title "<a href="/wiki/Devanampriya" title="Devanampriya">Devanaṃpiyena</a> <a href="/wiki/Priyadasi" title="Priyadasi">Piyadasi</a>" (𑀤𑁂𑀯𑀸𑀦𑀁𑀧𑀺𑀬𑁂𑀦 𑀧𑀺𑀬𑀤𑀲𑀺) in the <a href="/wiki/Lumbini" title="Lumbini">Lumbini</a> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Minor_Pillar_Edict" title="Minor Pillar Edict">Minor Pillar Edict</a>.</div></div></div></div></div>
<p>The name "A-shoka" literally means "without sorrow". According to an <i><a href="/wiki/Ashokavadana" title="Ashokavadana">Ashokavadana</a></i> legend, his mother gave him this name because his birth removed her sorrows.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEStrong1989205_38-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEStrong1989205-38">[37]</a></sup>
</p><p>The name <a href="/wiki/Priyadasi" title="Priyadasi">Priyadasi</a> is associated with Ashoka in the 3rd–4th century CE <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Dipavamsa" title="Dipavamsa">Dipavamsa</a></i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAllen201279_39-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAllen201279-39">[38]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-40"><a href="#cite_note-40">[39]</a></sup> The term literally means "he who regards amiably", or "of gracious mien" (<a href="/wiki/Sanskrit" title="Sanskrit">Sanskrit</a>: Priya-darshi). It may have been a <a href="/wiki/Regnal_name" title="Regnal name">regnal name</a> adopted by Ashoka.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEThapar1961226–227_41-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEThapar1961226–227-41">[40]</a></sup> <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Asokan_studies_42-0"><a href="#cite_note-Asokan_studies-42">[41]</a></sup> A version of this name is used for Ashoka in Greek-language inscriptions: βασιλεὺς Πιοδασσης ("<a href="/wiki/Basileus" title="Basileus">Basileus</a> Piodassēs").<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Asokan_studies_42-1"><a href="#cite_note-Asokan_studies-42">[41]</a></sup>
</p><p>Ashoka's inscriptions mention his title <i><a href="/wiki/Devanampriya" title="Devanampriya">Devanampiya</a></i> (Sanskrit: <i>Devanampriya</i>, "Beloved of the Gods"). The identification of Devanampiya and Ashoka as the same person is established by the <a href="/wiki/Maski#Minor_Rock_Edict_of_Ashoka" title="Maski">Maski</a> and Gujarra inscriptions, which use both these terms for the king.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEStrong198911_43-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEStrong198911-43">[42]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELahiri2015129_44-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELahiri2015129-44">[43]</a></sup> The title was adopted by other kings, including the contemporary king <a href="/wiki/Devanampiya_Tissa_of_Anuradhapura" title="Devanampiya Tissa of Anuradhapura">Devanampiya Tissa of Anuradhapura</a> and Ashoka's descendant <a href="/wiki/Dasharatha_Maurya" title="Dasharatha Maurya">Dasharatha Maurya</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEThapar1961226_45-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEThapar1961226-45">[44]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Date">Date</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ashoka&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Date">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Khalsi_rock_edict_of_Ashoka.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="4522" data-file-width="2611" decoding="async" height="381" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/89/Khalsi_rock_edict_of_Ashoka.jpg/220px-Khalsi_rock_edict_of_Ashoka.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/89/Khalsi_rock_edict_of_Ashoka.jpg/330px-Khalsi_rock_edict_of_Ashoka.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/89/Khalsi_rock_edict_of_Ashoka.jpg/440px-Khalsi_rock_edict_of_Ashoka.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Khalsi_rock_edict_of_Ashoka.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Major_Rock_Edict" title="Major Rock Edict">Major Rock Edict No.13</a> of Ashoka, mentions the Greek kings <a href="/wiki/Antiochus_II_Theos" title="Antiochus II Theos">Antiochus</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ptolemy_II_of_Egypt" title="Ptolemy II of Egypt">Ptolemy</a>, <a href="/wiki/Antigonus_II_Gonatas" title="Antigonus II Gonatas">Antigonus</a>, <a href="/wiki/Magas_of_Cyrene" title="Magas of Cyrene">Magas</a> and <a href="/wiki/Alexander_II_of_Epirus" title="Alexander II of Epirus">Alexander</a> by name, as recipients of his teachings.</div></div></div>
<p>The exact date of Ashoka's birth is not certain, as the extant contemporary Indian texts did not record such details. It is known that he lived in the 3rd century BCE, as his inscriptions mention several contemporary rulers whose dates are known with more certainty, such as <a href="/wiki/Antiochus_II_Theos" title="Antiochus II Theos">Antiochus II Theos</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ptolemy_II_Philadelphus" title="Ptolemy II Philadelphus">Ptolemy II Philadelphus</a>, <a href="/wiki/Antigonus_II_Gonatas" title="Antigonus II Gonatas">Antigonus II Gonatas</a>, <a href="/wiki/Magas_of_Cyrene" title="Magas of Cyrene">Magas of Cyrene</a>, and Alexander (of <a href="/wiki/Alexander_II_of_Epirus" title="Alexander II of Epirus">Epirus</a> or <a href="/wiki/Alexander_of_Corinth" title="Alexander of Corinth">Corinth</a>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELahiri201525_46-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELahiri201525-46">[45]</a></sup> Thus, Ashoka must have been born sometime in the late 4th century BCE or early 3rd century BCE (<abbr title="circa">c.</abbr><span style="white-space:nowrap;"> 304 BCE</span>),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELahiri201524_47-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELahiri201524-47">[46]</a></sup>
</p>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1096954695/mw-parser-output/.tmulti" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="thumb tmulti tright"><div class="thumbinner multiimageinner" style="width:392px;max-width:392px"><div class="trow"><div class="theader">Pataliputra at the time of Ashoka</div></div><div class="trow"><div class="tsingle" style="width:229px;max-width:229px"><div class="thumbimage" style="height:142px;overflow:hidden"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Mauryan_ruins_of_pillared_hall_at_Kumrahar_site_of_Pataliputra_ASIEC_1912-13.jpg"><img alt="" data-file-height="548" data-file-width="876" decoding="async" height="142" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1e/Mauryan_ruins_of_pillared_hall_at_Kumrahar_site_of_Pataliputra_ASIEC_1912-13.jpg/341px-Mauryan_ruins_of_pillared_hall_at_Kumrahar_site_of_Pataliputra_ASIEC_1912-13.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1e/Mauryan_ruins_of_pillared_hall_at_Kumrahar_site_of_Pataliputra_ASIEC_1912-13.jpg/454px-Mauryan_ruins_of_pillared_hall_at_Kumrahar_site_of_Pataliputra_ASIEC_1912-13.jpg 2x" width="227"/></a></div><div class="thumbcaption text-align-center">Ruins of pillared hall at <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Kumrahar" title="Kumrahar">Kumrahar</a> site at Pataliputra.</div></div><div class="tsingle" style="width:159px;max-width:159px"><div class="thumbimage" style="height:142px;overflow:hidden"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Pataliputra_capital,_Bihar_Museum,_Patna,_3rd_century_BCE.jpg"><img alt="" data-file-height="2815" data-file-width="3104" decoding="async" height="142" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/eb/Pataliputra_capital%2C_Bihar_Museum%2C_Patna%2C_3rd_century_BCE.jpg/157px-Pataliputra_capital%2C_Bihar_Museum%2C_Patna%2C_3rd_century_BCE.jpg" 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/eb/Pataliputra_capital%2C_Bihar_Museum%2C_Patna%2C_3rd_century_BCE.jpg/314px-Pataliputra_capital%2C_Bihar_Museum%2C_Patna%2C_3rd_century_BCE.jpg 2x" width="157"/></a></div><div class="thumbcaption text-align-center">The <a href="/wiki/Pataliputra_capital" title="Pataliputra capital">Pataliputra capital</a>, 4th–3rd c. BCE.</div></div></div><div class="trow" style="display:flow-root"><div class="thumbcaption" style="text-align:center">Ashoka was probably born in the city of <a href="/wiki/Pataliputra" title="Pataliputra">Pataliputra</a>. Remains of the city from around that time have been found through excavations in central areas of the modern city of <a href="/wiki/Patna" title="Patna">Patna</a>.</div></div></div></div>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Ancestry">Ancestry</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ashoka&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Ancestry">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Ashoka's own inscriptions are fairly detailed but make no mention of his ancestors.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELahiri201526_48-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELahiri201526-48">[47]</a></sup> Other sources, such as the <a href="/wiki/Puranas" title="Puranas">Puranas</a> and the <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mahavamsa" title="Mahavamsa">Mahavamsa</a></i> state that his father was the Mauryan emperor <a href="/wiki/Bindusara" title="Bindusara">Bindusara</a>, and his grandfather was <a href="/wiki/Chandragupta_Maurya" title="Chandragupta Maurya">Chandragupta</a> – the founder of the Empire.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEThapar196113_49-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEThapar196113-49">[48]</a></sup> The <i>Ashokavadana</i> also names his father as Bindusara, but traces his ancestry to Buddha's contemporary king <a href="/wiki/Bimbisara" title="Bimbisara">Bimbisara</a>, through <a href="/wiki/Ajatashatru" title="Ajatashatru">Ajatashatru</a>, <a href="/wiki/Udayin" title="Udayin">Udayin</a>, Munda, Kakavarnin, Sahalin, Tulakuchi, Mahamandala, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Prasenajit" title="Prasenajit">Prasenajit</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Nanda_Empire" title="Nanda Empire">Nanda</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEStrong1989204_50-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEStrong1989204-50">[49]</a></sup> The 16th century Tibetan monk <a href="/wiki/Taranatha" title="Taranatha">Taranatha</a>, whose account is a distorted version of the earlier traditions,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEThapar19619_35-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEThapar19619-35">[34]</a></sup> describes Ashoka as the illegitimate son of king Nemita of Champarana from the daughter of a merchant.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEThapar196125–26_51-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEThapar196125–26-51">[50]</a></sup>
</p><p><i>Ashokavadana</i> states that <a href="/wiki/Mother_of_Ashoka" title="Mother of Ashoka">Ashoka's mother</a> was the daughter of a Brahmin from <a href="/wiki/Champapuri" title="Champapuri">Champa</a>, and was prophesied to marry a king. Accordingly, her father took her to Pataliputra, where she was inducted into Bindusara's harem, and ultimately, became his chief queen.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEStrong1989204–205_52-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEStrong1989204–205-52">[51]</a></sup> The <i>Ashokavadana</i> does not mention her by name,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELahiri2015323_53-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELahiri2015323-53">[52]</a></sup> although other legends provide different names for her.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELahiri201531_54-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELahiri201531-54">[53]</a></sup> For example, the <i>Asokavadanamala</i> calls her Subhadrangi.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGuruge199319_55-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGuruge199319-55">[54]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMookerji19622_56-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMookerji19622-56">[55]</a></sup> The <i>Vamsatthapakasini</i> or <i>Mahavamsa-tika</i>, a commentary on <i>Mahavamsa</i>, calls her "Dharma" ("Dhamma" in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pali_language" title="Pali language">Pali</a>), and states that she belonged to the Moriya <a href="/wiki/Kshatriya" title="Kshatriya">Kshatriya</a> clan.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMookerji19622_56-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMookerji19622-56">[55]</a></sup> A <i><a href="/wiki/Divyavadana" title="Divyavadana">Divyavadana</a></i> legend calls her Janapada-kalyani;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESingh2008332_57-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESingh2008332-57">[56]</a></sup> according to scholar <a href="/wiki/Ananda_W._P._Guruge" title="Ananda W. P. Guruge">Ananda W. P. Guruge</a>, this is not a name, but an epithet.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGuruge199319_55-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGuruge199319-55">[54]</a></sup>
</p><p>According to the 2nd-century historian <a href="/wiki/Appian" title="Appian">Appian</a>, Chandragupta entered into a marital alliance with the Greek ruler <a href="/wiki/Seleucus_I_Nicator" title="Seleucus I Nicator">Seleucus I Nicator</a>, which has led to speculation that either Chandragupta or his son Bindusara married a Greek princess. However, there is no evidence that Ashoka's mother or grandmother was Greek, and most historians have dismissed the idea.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEThapar196120_58-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEThapar196120-58">[57]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="As_a_prince">As a prince</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ashoka&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: As a prince">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Ashoka's own inscriptions do not describe his early life, and much of the information on this topic comes from apocryphal legends written hundreds of years after him.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELahiri201527_59-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELahiri201527-59">[58]</a></sup> While these legends include obviously fictitious details such as narratives of Ashoka's past lives, they have some plausible historical information about Ashoka's period.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELahiri201527_59-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELahiri201527-59">[58]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESingh2008332_57-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESingh2008332-57">[56]</a></sup>
</p><p>According to the <i>Ashokavadana</i>, Bindusara disliked Ashoka because of his rough skin. One day, Bindusara asked the ascetic Pingala-vatsajiva to determine which of his sons was worthy of being his successor. He asked all the princes to assemble at the Garden of the Golden Pavilion on the ascetic's advice. Ashoka was reluctant to go because his father disliked him, but his mother convinced him to do so. When minister Radhagupta saw Ashoka leaving the capital for the Garden, he offered to provide the prince with a royal elephant for the travel.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEStrong1989206_60-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEStrong1989206-60">[59]</a></sup> At the Garden, Pingala-vatsajiva examined the princes and realised that Ashoka would be the next king. To avoid annoying Bindusara, the ascetic refused to name the successor. Instead, he said that one who had the best mount, seat, drink, vessel and food would be the next king; each time, Ashoka declared that he met the criterion. Later, he told Ashoka's mother that her son would be the next king, and on her advice, left the kingdom to avoid Bindusara's wrath.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEStrong1989207_61-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEStrong1989207-61">[60]</a></sup>
</p><p>While legends suggest that Bindusara disliked Ashoka's ugly appearance, they also state that Bindusara gave him important responsibilities, such as suppressing a revolt in Takshashila (according to north Indian tradition) and governing Ujjain (according to Sri Lankan tradition). This suggests that Bindusara was impressed by the other qualities of the prince.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEThapar196121_62-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEThapar196121-62">[61]</a></sup> Another possibility is that he sent Ashoka to distant regions to keep him away from the imperial capital.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELahiri201565_63-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELahiri201565-63">[62]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Rebellion_at_Taxila">Rebellion at Taxila</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ashoka&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Rebellion at Taxila">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Aramaic_inscription_at_Taxila_Museum.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1655" data-file-width="657" decoding="async" height="428" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/57/Aramaic_inscription_at_Taxila_Museum.jpg/170px-Aramaic_inscription_at_Taxila_Museum.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/57/Aramaic_inscription_at_Taxila_Museum.jpg/255px-Aramaic_inscription_at_Taxila_Museum.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/57/Aramaic_inscription_at_Taxila_Museum.jpg/340px-Aramaic_inscription_at_Taxila_Museum.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Aramaic_inscription_at_Taxila_Museum.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The <a href="/wiki/Aramaic_Inscription_of_Taxila" title="Aramaic Inscription of Taxila">Aramaic Inscription of Taxila</a> probably mentions Ashoka.</div></div></div>
<p>According to the <i>Ashokavadana</i>, Bindusara dispatched prince Ashoka to suppress a rebellion in the city of <a href="/wiki/Taxila" title="Taxila">Takshashila</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEStrong1989208_64-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEStrong1989208-64">[63]</a></sup> (present-day <a href="/wiki/Bhir_Mound" title="Bhir Mound">Bhir Mound</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELahiri201566_65-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELahiri201566-65">[64]</a></sup> in Pakistan). This episode is not mentioned in the Sri Lankan tradition, which instead states that Bindusara sent Ashoka to govern Ujjain. Two other Buddhist texts – <i>Ashoka-sutra</i> and <i>Kunala-sutra</i> – state that Bindusara appointed Ashoka as a viceroy in <a href="/wiki/Gandhara" title="Gandhara">Gandhara</a> (where Takshashila was located), not Ujjain. <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEThapar196121_62-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEThapar196121-62">[61]</a></sup>
</p><p>The <i>Ashokavadana</i> states that Bindusara provided Ashoka with a fourfold-army (comprising cavalry, elephants, chariots and infantry) but refused to provide any weapons for this army. Ashoka declared that weapons would appear before him if he was worthy of being a king, and then, the deities emerged from the earth and provided weapons to the army. When Ashoka reached Takshashila, the citizens welcomed him and told him that their rebellion was only against the evil ministers, not the king. Sometime later, Ashoka was similarly welcomed in the <a href="/wiki/Khasas" title="Khasas">Khasa</a> territory and the gods declared that he would go on to conquer the whole earth.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEStrong1989208_64-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEStrong1989208-64">[63]</a></sup>
</p><p>Takshashila was a prosperous and geopolitically influential city, and historical evidence proves that by Ashoka's time, it was well-connected to the Mauryan capital Pataliputra by the <i>Uttarapatha</i> trade route.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELahiri201570_66-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELahiri201570-66">[65]</a></sup> However, no extant contemporary source mentions the Takshashila rebellion, and none of Ashoka's records states that he ever visited the city.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELahiri201566-67_67-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELahiri201566-67-67">[66]</a></sup> That said, the historicity of the legend about Ashoka's involvement in the Takshashila rebellion may be corroborated by <a href="/wiki/Aramaic_Inscription_of_Taxila" title="Aramaic Inscription of Taxila">an Aramaic-language inscription</a> discovered at <a href="/wiki/Sirkap" title="Sirkap">Sirkap</a> near Taxila. The inscription includes a name that begins with the letters "prydr", and most scholars restore it as "Priyadarshi", which was the title of Ashoka.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEThapar196121_62-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEThapar196121-62">[61]</a></sup> Another evidence of Ashoka's connection to the city may be the name of the <a href="/wiki/Dharmarajika_Stupa" title="Dharmarajika Stupa">Dharmarajika Stupa</a> near Taxila; the name suggests that it was built by Ashoka ("Dharma-raja"). <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELahiri201568_68-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELahiri201568-68">[67]</a></sup>
</p><p>The story about the deities miraculously bringing weapons to Ashoka may be the text's way of deifying Ashoka; or indicating that Bindusara – who disliked Ashoka – wanted him to fail in Takshashila. <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELahiri201567_69-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELahiri201567-69">[68]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Governor_of_Ujjain">Governor of Ujjain</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ashoka&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Governor of Ujjain">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>According to the <i>Mahavamsa</i>, Bindusara appointed Ashoka as the viceroy of present-day <a href="/wiki/Ujjain" title="Ujjain">Ujjain</a> (Ujjeni),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEThapar196121_62-3"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEThapar196121-62">[61]</a></sup> which was an important administrative and commercial centre in the Avanti province of central India.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELahiri201589–90_70-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELahiri201589–90-70">[69]</a></sup> This tradition is corroborated by the <a href="/wiki/Saru_Maru" title="Saru Maru">Saru Maru</a> inscription discovered in central India; this inscription states that he visited the place as a prince.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAllen2012154_71-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAllen2012154-71">[70]</a></sup> Ashoka's own <a href="/wiki/Major_Rock_Edicts#First_Separate_Major_Rock_Edict" title="Major Rock Edicts">rock edict</a> mentions the presence of a prince viceroy at Ujjain during his reign,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGuruge199328_72-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGuruge199328-72">[71]</a></sup> which further supports the tradition that he himself served as a viceroy at Ujjain.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELahiri201598_73-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELahiri201598-73">[72]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Panguraria_Top_View_Closeup_detail.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2301" data-file-width="2150" decoding="async" height="182" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9f/Panguraria_Top_View_Closeup_detail.jpg/170px-Panguraria_Top_View_Closeup_detail.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9f/Panguraria_Top_View_Closeup_detail.jpg/255px-Panguraria_Top_View_Closeup_detail.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9f/Panguraria_Top_View_Closeup_detail.jpg/340px-Panguraria_Top_View_Closeup_detail.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Panguraria_Top_View_Closeup_detail.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The <a href="/wiki/Saru_Maru" title="Saru Maru">Saru Maru commemorative inscription</a> seems to mention the presence of Ashoka in the area of Ujjain as he was still a Prince.</div></div></div>
<p>Pataliputra was connected to Ujjain by multiple routes in Ashoka's time, and on the way, Ashoka entourage may have encamped at Rupnath, where his inscription has been found.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELahiri201594–95_74-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELahiri201594–95-74">[73]</a></sup>
</p><p>According to the Sri Lankan tradition, Ashoka visited <a href="/wiki/Vidisha" title="Vidisha">Vidisha</a>, where he fell in love with a beautiful woman on his way to Ujjain. According to the <i>Dipamvamsa</i> and <i>Mahamvamsa</i>, the woman was <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Devi_(Ashoka%27s_wife)" title="Devi (Ashoka's wife)">Devi</a> – the daughter of a merchant. According to the <i>Mahabodhi-vamsa</i>, she was Vidisha-Mahadevi and belonged to the <a href="/wiki/Shakya" title="Shakya">Shakya</a> clan of <a href="/wiki/Gautama_Buddha" title="Gautama Buddha">Gautama Buddha</a>. The Buddhist chroniclers may have fabricated the Shakya connection to connect Ashoka's family to Buddha.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEThapar196122–23_75-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEThapar196122–23-75">[74]</a></sup> The Buddhist texts allude to her being a Buddhist in her later years but do not describe her conversion to Buddhism. Therefore, it is likely that she was already a Buddhist when she met Ashoka.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELahiri2015101_76-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELahiri2015101-76">[75]</a></sup>
</p><p>The <i>Mahavamsa</i> states that Devi gave birth to Ashoka's son <a href="/wiki/Mahinda_(Buddhist_monk)" title="Mahinda (Buddhist monk)">Mahinda</a> in Ujjain, and two years later, to a daughter named <a href="/wiki/Sanghamitta" title="Sanghamitta">Sanghamitta</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELahiri201597_77-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELahiri201597-77">[76]</a></sup> According to the <i>Mahavamsa</i>, Ashoka's son Mahinda was ordained at the age of 20 years, during the sixth year of Ashoka's reign. That means Mahinda must have been 14 years old when Ashoka ascended the throne. Even if Mahinda was born when Ashoka was as young as 20 years old, Ashoka must have ascended the throne at 34 years, which means he must have served as a viceroy for several years.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEThapar196124–25_78-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEThapar196124–25-78">[77]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Ascension_to_the_throne">Ascension to the throne</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ashoka&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Ascension to the throne">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Legends suggest that Ashoka was not the crown prince, and his ascension on the throne was disputed. <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEThapar196125_79-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEThapar196125-79">[78]</a></sup>
</p><p><i>Ashokavadana</i> states that Bindusara's eldest son Susima once slapped a bald minister on his head in jest. The minister worried that after ascending the throne, <a href="/wiki/Susima" title="Susima">Susima</a> may jokingly hurt him with a sword. Therefore, he instigated five hundred ministers to support Ashoka's claim to the throne when the time came, noting that Ashoka was predicted to become a <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Chakravartin" title="Chakravartin">chakravartin</a></i> (universal ruler).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELahiri2015102_80-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELahiri2015102-80">[79]</a></sup> Sometime later, Takshashila rebelled again, and Bindusara dispatched Susima to curb the rebellion. Shortly after, Bindusara fell ill and was expected to die soon. Susima was still in Takshashila, having been unsuccessful in suppressing the rebellion. Bindusara recalled him to the capital and asked Ashoka to march to Takshashila.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEStrong1989209_81-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEStrong1989209-81">[80]</a></sup> However, the ministers told him that Ashoka was ill and suggested that he temporarily install Ashoka on the throne until Susmia's return from Takshashila.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELahiri2015102_80-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELahiri2015102-80">[79]</a></sup> When Bindusara refused to do so, Ashoka declared that if the throne were rightfully his, the gods would crown him as the next king. At that instance, the gods did so, Bindusara died, and Ashoka's authority extended to the entire world, including the <a href="/wiki/Yaksha" title="Yaksha">Yaksha</a> territory located above the earth and the <a href="/wiki/N%C4%81ga" title="Nāga">Naga</a> territory located below the earth.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEStrong1989209_81-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEStrong1989209-81">[80]</a></sup> When Susima returned to the capital, Ashoka's newly appointed prime minister Radhagupta tricked him into a pit of charcoal. Susima died a painful death, and his general Bhadrayudha became a Buddhist monk.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEStrong1989210_82-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEStrong1989210-82">[81]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Sarnath_Ashoka_Lions_with_Dharma_Wheel.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1973" data-file-width="793" decoding="async" height="547" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e1/Sarnath_Ashoka_Lions_with_Dharma_Wheel.jpg/220px-Sarnath_Ashoka_Lions_with_Dharma_Wheel.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e1/Sarnath_Ashoka_Lions_with_Dharma_Wheel.jpg/330px-Sarnath_Ashoka_Lions_with_Dharma_Wheel.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e1/Sarnath_Ashoka_Lions_with_Dharma_Wheel.jpg/440px-Sarnath_Ashoka_Lions_with_Dharma_Wheel.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Sarnath_Ashoka_Lions_with_Dharma_Wheel.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The <a href="/wiki/Lion_Capital_of_Ashoka" title="Lion Capital of Ashoka">Lion Capital of Ashoka</a> in <a href="/wiki/Sarnath" title="Sarnath">Sarnath</a>, showing its four <a href="/wiki/Asiatic_lion" title="Asiatic lion">Asiatic lions</a> standing back to back, and symbolizing the <a href="/wiki/Four_Noble_Truths" title="Four Noble Truths">Four Noble Truths</a> of <a href="/wiki/Buddhism" title="Buddhism">Buddhism</a>, supporting the Wheel of Moral law (<a href="/wiki/Dharmachakra" title="Dharmachakra">Dharmachakra</a>, reconstitution per Sarnath Museum notice).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAllen201215_83-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAllen201215-83">[82]</a></sup> The lions stand on a circular <a href="/wiki/Abacus_(architecture)" title="Abacus (architecture)">abacus</a>, decorated with dharmachakras alternating with four animals in profile: horse, bull, elephant, and lion. The architectural <i>bell</i> below the abacus, is a stylized upside down lotus. <a href="/wiki/Sarnath_Museum" title="Sarnath Museum">Sarnath Museum</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-84"><a href="#cite_note-84">[83]</a></sup></div></div></div>
<p>The <i>Mahavamsa</i> states that when Bindusara fell sick, Ashoka returned to Pataliputra from Ujjain and gained control of the capital. After his father's death, Ashoka had his eldest brother killed and ascended the throne.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELahiri2015101_76-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELahiri2015101-76">[75]</a></sup> The text also states that Ashoka killed ninety-nine of his half-brothers, including Sumana.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGuruge199328_72-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGuruge199328-72">[71]</a></sup> The <i>Dipavamsa</i> states that he killed a hundred of his brothers and was crowned four years later.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELahiri2015102_80-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELahiri2015102-80">[79]</a></sup> The <i>Vamsatthapakasini</i> adds that an Ajivika ascetic had predicted this massacre based on the interpretation of a dream of Ashoka's mother.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEThapar196126_85-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEThapar196126-85">[84]</a></sup> According to these accounts, only Ashoka's uterine brother Tissa was spared.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEThapar196127_86-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEThapar196127-86">[85]</a></sup> Other sources name the surviving brother Vitashoka, Vigatashoka, Sudatta (So-ta-to in <i>A-yi-uang-chuan</i>), or Sugatra (Siu-ka-tu-lu in <i>Fen-pie-kung-te-hun</i>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEThapar196127_86-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEThapar196127-86">[85]</a></sup>
</p><p>The figures such as 99 and 100 are exaggerated and seem to be a way of stating that Ashoka killed several of his brothers.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELahiri2015102_80-3"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELahiri2015102-80">[79]</a></sup> Taranatha states that Ashoka, who was an illegitimate son of his predecessor, killed six legitimate princes to ascend the throne.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEThapar196125–26_51-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEThapar196125–26-51">[50]</a></sup> It is possible that Ashoka was not the rightful heir to the throne and killed a brother (or brothers) to acquire the throne. However, the Buddhist sources have exaggerated the story, which attempts to portray him as evil before his conversion to Buddhism. Ashoka's Rock Edict No. 5 mentions officers whose duties include supervising the welfare of "the families of his brothers, sisters, and other relatives". This suggests that more than one of his brothers survived his ascension. However, some scholars oppose this suggestion, arguing that the inscription talks only about the <i>families</i> of his brothers, not the brothers themselves.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEThapar196127_86-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEThapar196127-86">[85]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Date_of_ascension">Date of ascension</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ashoka&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Date of ascension">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>According to the Sri Lankan texts <i>Mahavamsa</i> and the <i>Dipavamsa</i>, Ashoka ascended the throne 218 years after the death of Gautama Buddha and ruled for 37 years.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEThapar196113–14_87-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEThapar196113–14-87">[86]</a></sup> The date of the Buddha's death is itself a matter of debate,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEThapar196114_88-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEThapar196114-88">[87]</a></sup> and the North Indian tradition states that Ashoka ruled a hundred years after the Buddha's death, which has led to further debates about the date.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEStrong1995144_29-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEStrong1995144-29">[28]</a></sup>
</p><p>Assuming that the Sri Lankan tradition is correct, and assuming that the Buddha died in 483 BCE – a date proposed by several scholars – Ashoka must have ascended the throne in 265 BCE.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEThapar196114_88-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEThapar196114-88">[87]</a></sup> The Puranas state that Ashoka's father Bindusara reigned for 25 years, not 28 years as specified in the Sri Lankan tradition.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEThapar196113_49-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEThapar196113-49">[48]</a></sup> If this is true, Ashoka's ascension can be dated three years earlier, to 268 BCE. Alternatively, if the Sri Lankan tradition is correct, but if we assume that the Buddha died in 486 BCE (a date supported by the Cantonese Dotted Record), Ashoka's ascension can be dated to 268 BCE.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEThapar196114_88-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEThapar196114-88">[87]</a></sup> The <i>Mahavamsa</i> states that Ashoka consecrated himself as the king four years after becoming a sovereign. This interregnum can be explained assuming that he fought a war of succession with other sons of Bindusara during these four years.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEThapar196130_89-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEThapar196130-89">[88]</a></sup>
</p><p>The <i>Ashokavadana</i> contains a story about Ashoka's minister Yashas hiding the sun with his hand. Professor P. H. L. Eggermont theorised that this story was a reference to a partial solar eclipse that was seen in northern India on 4 May 249 BCE.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEStrong198912–13_90-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEStrong198912–13-90">[89]</a></sup> According to the <i>Ashokavadana</i>, Ashoka went on a pilgrimage to various Buddhist sites sometime after this eclipse. Ashoka's Rummindei pillar inscription states that he visited <a href="/wiki/Lumbini" title="Lumbini">Lumbini</a> during his 21st regnal year. Assuming this visit was a part of the pilgrimage described in the text, and assuming that Ashoka visited Lumbini around 1–2 years after the solar eclipse, the ascension date of 268–269 BCE seems more likely.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEThapar196114_88-3"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEThapar196114-88">[87]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELahiri201525_46-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELahiri201525-46">[45]</a></sup> However, this theory is not universally accepted. For example, according to <a href="/wiki/John_S._Strong" title="John S. Strong">John S. Strong</a>, the event described in the <i>Ashokavadana</i> has nothing to do with chronology, and Eggermont's interpretation grossly ignores the literary and religious context of the legend.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEStrong198913_91-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEStrong198913-91">[90]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Reign_before_Buddhist_influence">Reign before Buddhist influence</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ashoka&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Reign before Buddhist influence">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Both Sri Lankan and North Indian traditions assert that Ashoka was a violent person before Buddhism.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGuruge,_Unresolved199546_92-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGuruge,_Unresolved199546-92">[91]</a></sup> Taranatha also states that Ashoka was initially called "Kamashoka" because he spent many years in pleasurable pursuits (<i><a href="/wiki/Kama" title="Kama">kama</a></i>); he was then called "Chandashoka" ("Ashoka the fierce") because he spent some years performing evil deeds; and finally, he came to be known as Dhammashoka ("Ashoka the righteous") after his conversion to Buddhism.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEThapar196129_93-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEThapar196129-93">[92]</a></sup>
</p><p>The <i>Ashokavadana</i> also calls him "Chandashoka", and describes several of his cruel acts:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELahiri2015105_94-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELahiri2015105-94">[93]</a></sup>
</p>
<ul><li>The ministers who had helped him ascend the throne started treating him with contempt after his ascension. To test their loyalty, Ashoka gave them the absurd order of cutting down every flower-and fruit-bearing tree. When they failed to carry out this order, Ashoka personally cut off the heads of 500 ministers.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELahiri2015105_94-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELahiri2015105-94">[93]</a></sup></li>
<li>One day, during a stroll at a park, Ashoka and his concubines came across a beautiful <a href="/wiki/Saraca_asoca" title="Saraca asoca">Ashoka tree</a>. The sight put him in an amourous mood, but the women did not enjoy caressing his rough skin. Sometime later, when Ashoka fell asleep, the resentful women chopped the flowers and the branches of his namesake tree. After Ashoka woke up, he burnt 500 of his concubines to death as punishment.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELahiri2015106_95-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELahiri2015106-95">[94]</a></sup></li>
<li>Alarmed by the king's involvement in such massacres, prime minister Radha-Gupta proposed hiring an executioner to carry out future mass killings to leave the king unsullied. Girika, a Magadha village boy who boasted that he could execute the whole of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Jambudvipa" title="Jambudvipa">Jambudvipa</a>, was hired for the purpose. He came to be known as Chandagirika ("Girika the fierce"), and on his request, Ashoka built a jail in Pataliputra.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELahiri2015106_95-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELahiri2015106-95">[94]</a></sup> Called <a href="/wiki/Ashoka%27s_Hell" title="Ashoka's Hell">Ashoka's Hell</a>, the jail looked pleasant from the outside, but inside it, Girika brutally tortured the prisoners.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELahiri2015106–107_96-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELahiri2015106–107-96">[95]</a></sup></li></ul>
<p>The 5th-century Chinese traveller <a href="/wiki/Faxian" title="Faxian">Faxian</a> states that Ashoka personally visited the underworld to study torture methods there and then invented his methods. The 7th-century traveller <a href="/wiki/Xuanzang" title="Xuanzang">Xuanzang</a> claims to have seen a pillar marking the site of Ashoka's "Hell".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEThapar196129_93-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEThapar196129-93">[92]</a></sup>
</p><p>The <i>Mahavamsa</i> also briefly alludes to Ashoka's cruelty, stating that Ashoka was earlier called Chandashoka because of his evil deeds but came to be called Dharmashoka because of his pious acts after his conversion to Buddhism.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELahiri2015107_97-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELahiri2015107-97">[96]</a></sup> However, unlike the north Indian tradition, the Sri Lankan texts do not mention any specific evil deeds performed by Ashoka, except his killing of 99 of his brothers.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGuruge,_Unresolved199546_92-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGuruge,_Unresolved199546-92">[91]</a></sup>
</p><p>Such descriptions of Ashoka as an evil person before his conversion to Buddhism appear to be a fabrication of the Buddhist authors,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEThapar196129_93-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEThapar196129-93">[92]</a></sup> who attempted to present the change that Buddhism brought to him as a miracle.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGuruge,_Unresolved199546_92-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGuruge,_Unresolved199546-92">[91]</a></sup> In an attempt to dramatise this change, such legends exaggerate Ashoka's past wickedness and his piousness after the conversion.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Charles1962_98-0"><a href="#cite_note-Charles1962-98">[97]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Kalinga_war_and_conversion_to_Buddhism">Kalinga war and conversion to Buddhism</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ashoka&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Kalinga war and conversion to Buddhism">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Kalinga_war" title="Kalinga war">Kalinga war</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Kanaganahalli_inscribed_panel_portraying_Asoka_(perspective).jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1495" data-file-width="1213" decoding="async" height="271" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/99/Kanaganahalli_inscribed_panel_portraying_Asoka_%28perspective%29.jpg/220px-Kanaganahalli_inscribed_panel_portraying_Asoka_%28perspective%29.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/99/Kanaganahalli_inscribed_panel_portraying_Asoka_%28perspective%29.jpg/330px-Kanaganahalli_inscribed_panel_portraying_Asoka_%28perspective%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/99/Kanaganahalli_inscribed_panel_portraying_Asoka_%28perspective%29.jpg/440px-Kanaganahalli_inscribed_panel_portraying_Asoka_%28perspective%29.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Kanaganahalli_inscribed_panel_portraying_Asoka_(perspective).jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Kanaganahalli" title="Kanaganahalli">Kanaganahalli</a> inscribed panel portraying Asoka with <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Brahmi" title="Brahmi">Brahmi</a> label "King Asoka", 1st–3rd century CE.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-99"><a href="#cite_note-99">[98]</a></sup></div></div></div>
