<p>Ashoka's inscriptions mention that he conquered the <a href="/wiki/Kalinga_(historical_region)" title="Kalinga (historical region)">Kalinga</a> region during his 8th regnal year: the destruction caused during the war made him repent violence, and in the subsequent years, he was drawn towards Buddhism.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGuruge,_Unresolved199549–50_100-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGuruge,_Unresolved199549–50-100">[99]</a></sup> Edict 13 of the <a href="/wiki/Edicts_of_Ashoka" title="Edicts of Ashoka">Edicts of Ashoka</a> Rock Inscriptions expresses the great remorse the king felt after observing the destruction of Kalinga:
</p>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r996844942">.mw-parser-output .templatequote{overflow:hidden;margin:1em 0;padding:0 40px}.mw-parser-output .templatequote .templatequotecite{line-height:1.5em;text-align:left;padding-left:1.6em;margin-top:0}</style><blockquote class="templatequote"><p>Directly, after the Kalingas had been annexed, began His Sacred Majesty's zealous protection of the Law of Piety, his love of that Law, and his inculcation of that Law. Thence arises the remorse of His Sacred Majesty for having conquered the Kalingas because the conquest of a country previously unconquered involves the slaughter, death, and carrying away captive of the people. That is a matter of profound sorrow and regret to His Sacred Majesty.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Wikisource_Smith_101-0"><a href="#cite_note-Wikisource_Smith-101">[100]</a></sup></p></blockquote>
<p>On the other hand, the Sri Lankan tradition suggests that Ashoka was already a devoted Buddhist by his 8th regnal year, converted to Buddhism during his 4th regnal year, and constructed 84,000 viharas during his 5th–7th regnal years.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGuruge,_Unresolved199549–50_100-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGuruge,_Unresolved199549–50-100">[99]</a></sup> The Buddhist legends make no mention of the Kalinga campaign.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEThapar199518_102-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEThapar199518-102">[101]</a></sup>
</p><p>Based on Sri Lankan tradition, some scholars, such as Eggermont, believe Ashoka converted to Buddhism <i>before</i> the Kalinga war.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEThapar196136_103-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEThapar196136-103">[102]</a></sup> Critics of this theory argue that if Ashoka were already a Buddhist, he would not have waged the violent Kalinga War. Eggermont explains this anomaly by theorising that Ashoka had his own interpretation of the "<a href="/wiki/Middle_Way" title="Middle Way">Middle Way</a>".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEThapar196133_104-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEThapar196133-104">[103]</a></sup>
</p><p>Some earlier writers believed that Ashoka <i>dramatically</i> converted to Buddhism after seeing the suffering caused by the war since his Major Rock Edict 13 states that he became closer to the dhamma after the annexation of Kalinga.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEThapar199518_102-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEThapar199518-102">[101]</a></sup> However, even if Ashoka converted to Buddhism <i>after</i> the war, epigraphic evidence suggests that his conversion was a <i>gradual</i> process rather than a dramatic event.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEThapar199518_102-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEThapar199518-102">[101]</a></sup> For example, in a Minor Rock Edict issued during his 13th regnal year (five years after the Kalinga campaign), he states that he had been an <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Upasaka" title="Upasaka">upasaka</a></i> (lay Buddhist) for more than two and a half years, but did not make much progress; in the past year, he was drawn closer to the sangha and became a more ardent follower.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEThapar199518_102-3"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEThapar199518-102">[101]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="The_Kalinga_War">The Kalinga War</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ashoka&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: The Kalinga War">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>According to Ashoka's Major Rock Edict 13, he conquered Kalinga 8 years after ascending to the throne. The edict states that during his conquest of Kalinga, 100,000 men and animals were killed in action; many times that number "perished"; and 150,000 men and animals were carried away from Kalinga as captives. Ashoka states that the repentance of these sufferings caused him to devote himself to the practice and propagation of dharma.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGuruge,_Unresolved199538_105-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGuruge,_Unresolved199538-105">[104]</a></sup> He proclaims that he now considered the slaughter, death and deportation caused during the conquest of a country painful and deplorable; and that he considered the suffering caused to the religious people and householders even more deplorable.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGuruge,_Unresolved199538_105-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGuruge,_Unresolved199538-105">[104]</a></sup>
</p><p>This edict has been inscribed at several places, including Erragudi, Girnar, Kalsi, Maneshra, Shahbazgarhi and Kandahar.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGuruge,_Unresolved199537_106-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGuruge,_Unresolved199537-106">[105]</a></sup> However, it is omitted in Ashoka's inscriptions found in the Kalinga region, where the Rock Edicts 13 and 14 have been replaced by two separate edicts that make no mention of Ashoka's remorse. It is possible that Ashoka did not consider it politically appropriate to make such a confession to the people of Kalinga.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEThapar199530–31_107-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEThapar199530–31-107">[106]</a></sup> Another possibility is the Kalinga war and its consequences, as described in Ashoka's rock edicts, are "more imaginary than real". This description is meant to impress those far removed from the scene, thus unable to verify its accuracy.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGuruge,_Unresolved199556_108-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGuruge,_Unresolved199556-108">[107]</a></sup>
</p><p>Ancient sources do not mention any other military activity of Ashoka, although the 16th-century writer <a href="/wiki/Taranatha" title="Taranatha">Taranatha</a> claims that Ashoka conquered the entire Jambudvipa.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEThapar196136_103-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEThapar196136-103">[102]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="First_contact_with_Buddhism">First contact with Buddhism</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ashoka&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: First contact with Buddhism">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Different sources give different accounts of Ashoka's conversion to Buddhism. <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEThapar196129_93-3"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEThapar196129-93">[92]</a></sup>
</p><p>According to Sri Lankan tradition, Ashoka's father, Bindusara, was a devotee of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Brahmanism" title="Brahmanism">Brahmanism</a>, and his mother Dharma was a devotee of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ajivika" title="Ajivika">Ajivikas</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGuruge,_Unresolved199542_109-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGuruge,_Unresolved199542-109">[108]</a></sup> The <i><a href="/wiki/Samantapasadika" title="Samantapasadika">Samantapasadika</a></i> states that Ashoka followed non-Buddhist sects during the first three years of his reign.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGuruge,_Unresolved199543_110-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGuruge,_Unresolved199543-110">[109]</a></sup> The Sri Lankan texts add that Ashoka was not happy with the behaviour of the Brahmins who received his alms daily. His courtiers produced some Ajivika and Nigantha teachers before him, but these also failed to impress him. <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGuruge,_Unresolved199547_111-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGuruge,_Unresolved199547-111">[110]</a></sup>
</p><p>The <i>Dipavamsa</i> states that Ashoka invited several non-Buddhist religious leaders to his palace and bestowed great gifts upon them in the hope that they would answer a question posed by the king. The text does not state what the question was but mentions that none of the invitees were able to answer it.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELahiri2015109_112-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELahiri2015109-112">[111]</a></sup> One day, Ashoka saw a young Buddhist monk called Nigrodha (or Nyagrodha), who was looking for alms on a road in Pataliputra.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELahiri2015109_112-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELahiri2015109-112">[111]</a></sup> He was the king's nephew, although the king was not aware of this:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGombrich19957_113-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGombrich19957-113">[112]</a></sup> he was a posthumous son of Ashoka's eldest brother Sumana, whom Ashoka had killed during the conflict for the throne.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEThapar196134_114-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEThapar196134-114">[113]</a></sup> Ashoka was impressed by Nigrodha's tranquil and fearless appearance, and asked him to teach him his faith. In response, Nigrodha offered him a sermon on <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Appamada" title="Appamada">appamada</a> (earnestness).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELahiri2015109_112-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELahiri2015109-112">[111]</a></sup> Impressed by the sermon, Ashoka offered Nigrodha 400,000 silver coins and 8 daily portions of rice.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELahiri2015110_115-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELahiri2015110-115">[114]</a></sup> The king became a Buddhist <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Upasaka" title="Upasaka">upasaka</a>, and started visiting the <a href="/wiki/Kukkutarama" title="Kukkutarama">Kukkutarama</a> shrine at Pataliputra. At the temple, he met the Buddhist monk <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Moggaliputta_Tissa" title="Moggaliputta Tissa">Moggaliputta Tissa</a>, and became more devoted to the Buddhist faith.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGuruge,_Unresolved199547_111-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGuruge,_Unresolved199547-111">[110]</a></sup> The veracity of this story is not certain.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELahiri2015110_115-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELahiri2015110-115">[114]</a></sup> This legend about Ashoka's search for a worthy teacher may be aimed at explaining why Ashoka did not adopt <a href="/wiki/Jainism" title="Jainism">Jainism</a>, another major contemporary faith that advocates non-violence and compassion. The legend suggests that Ashoka was not attracted to Buddhism because he was looking for such a faith, rather, for a competent spiritual teacher.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELahiri2015108_116-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELahiri2015108-116">[115]</a></sup> The Sri Lankan tradition adds that during his sixth regnal year, Ashoka's son Mahinda became a Buddhist monk, and his daughter became a Buddhist nun.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGuruge,_Unresolved199549_117-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGuruge,_Unresolved199549-117">[116]</a></sup>
</p><p>A story in <i>Divyavadana</i> attributes Ashoka's conversion to the Buddhist monk Samudra, who was an ex-merchant from Shravasti. According to this account, Samudra was imprisoned in Ashoka's "Hell", but saved himself using his miraculous powers. When Ashoka heard about this, he visited the monk, and was further impressed by a series of miracles performed by the monk. He then became a Buddhist.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEThapar196135_118-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEThapar196135-118">[117]</a></sup> A story in the <i>Ashokavadana</i> states that Samudra was a merchant's son, and was a 12-year-old boy when he met Ashoka; this account seems to be influenced by the Nigrodha story.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEThapar196136_103-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEThapar196136-103">[102]</a></sup>
</p><p>The A-yu-wang-chuan states that a 7-year-old Buddhist converted Ashoka. Another story claims that the young boy ate 500 <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Brahmins" title="Brahmins">Brahmanas</a> who were harassing Ashoka for being interested in Buddhism; these Brahmanas later miraculously turned into Buddhist <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bhikku" title="Bhikku">bhikkus</a> at the Kukkutarama monastery, which Ashoka visited.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEThapar196135_118-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEThapar196135-118">[117]</a></sup>
</p><p>Several Buddhist establishments existed in various parts of India by the time of Ashoka's ascension. It is not clear which branch of the Buddhist sangha influenced him, but the one at his capital <a href="/wiki/Pataliputra" title="Pataliputra">Pataliputra</a> is a good candidate.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELahiri2015135_119-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELahiri2015135-119">[118]</a></sup> Another good candidate is the one at <a href="/wiki/Mahabodhi_Temple" title="Mahabodhi Temple">Mahabodhi</a>: the Major Rock Edict 8 records his visit to the <a href="/wiki/Bodhi_Tree" title="Bodhi Tree">Bodhi Tree</a> – the place of Buddha's enlightenment at Mahabodhi – after his tenth regnal year, and the minor rock edict issued during his 13th regnal year suggests that he had become a Buddhist around the same time.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELahiri2015135_119-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELahiri2015135-119">[118]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEThapar199518_102-4"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEThapar199518-102">[101]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Reign_after_Buddhist_influence">Reign after Buddhist influence</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ashoka&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Reign after Buddhist influence">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Construction_of_Stupas_and_Temples">Construction of Stupas and Temples</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ashoka&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Construction of Stupas and Temples">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Sanchi2.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="578" data-file-width="866" decoding="async" height="147" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c8/Sanchi2.jpg/220px-Sanchi2.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c8/Sanchi2.jpg/330px-Sanchi2.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c8/Sanchi2.jpg/440px-Sanchi2.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Sanchi2.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Stupa of <a href="/wiki/Sanchi" title="Sanchi">Sanchi</a>. The central stupa was built during the Mauryas, and enlarged during the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sungas" title="Sungas">Sungas</a>, but the decorative gateway is dated to the later dynasty of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Satavahanas" title="Satavahanas">Satavahanas</a>.</div></div></div>
<p>Both <i>Mahavamsa</i> and <i>Ashokavadana</i> state that Ashoka constructed 84,000 stupas or viharas.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEStrong1995154–155_120-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEStrong1995154–155-120">[119]</a></sup> According to the <i>Mahavamsa</i>, this activity took place during his fifth–seventh regnal years.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGuruge,_Unresolved199549_117-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGuruge,_Unresolved199549-117">[116]</a></sup>
</p><p>The <i>Ashokavadana</i> states that Ashoka collected seven out of the eight relics of Gautama Buddha, and had their portions kept in 84,000 boxes made of gold, silver, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cymophane" title="Cymophane">cat's eye</a>, and crystal. He ordered the construction of 84,000 stupas throughout the earth, in towns that had a population of 100,000 or more. He told Elder Yashas, a monk at the Kukkutarama monastery, that he wanted these stupas to be completed on the same day. Yashas stated that he would signal the completion time by eclipsing the sun with his hand. When he did so, the 84,000 stupas were completed at once.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEStrong1995155_31-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEStrong1995155-31">[30]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Bharhut_relief_with_Diamond_throne_and_Mahabodhi_Temple_around_the_Boddhi_Tree.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1754" data-file-width="1104" decoding="async" height="350" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7d/Bharhut_relief_with_Diamond_throne_and_Mahabodhi_Temple_around_the_Boddhi_Tree.jpg/220px-Bharhut_relief_with_Diamond_throne_and_Mahabodhi_Temple_around_the_Boddhi_Tree.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7d/Bharhut_relief_with_Diamond_throne_and_Mahabodhi_Temple_around_the_Boddhi_Tree.jpg/330px-Bharhut_relief_with_Diamond_throne_and_Mahabodhi_Temple_around_the_Boddhi_Tree.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7d/Bharhut_relief_with_Diamond_throne_and_Mahabodhi_Temple_around_the_Boddhi_Tree.jpg/440px-Bharhut_relief_with_Diamond_throne_and_Mahabodhi_Temple_around_the_Boddhi_Tree.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Bharhut_relief_with_Diamond_throne_and_Mahabodhi_Temple_around_the_Boddhi_Tree.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Illustration of the original <a href="/wiki/Mahabodhi_Temple" title="Mahabodhi Temple">Mahabodhi Temple</a> temple built by Asoka at <a href="/wiki/Bodh_Gaya" title="Bodh Gaya">Bodh Gaya</a>. At the center, the <i><a href="/wiki/Vajrasana,_Bodh_Gaya" title="Vajrasana, Bodh Gaya">Vajrasana</a></i>, or "Enlightenment Throne of the Buddha", with its supporting columns, being the object of adoration. A <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pillar_of_Ashoka" title="Pillar of Ashoka">Pillar of Ashoka</a> topped by an elephant appears in the right corner. <a href="/wiki/Bharhut" title="Bharhut">Bharhut</a> relief, 1st century BCE.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-121"><a href="#cite_note-121">[120]</a></sup></div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Vajrasana,_early_20th_century.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="508" data-file-width="919" decoding="async" height="122" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2b/Vajrasana%2C_early_20th_century.jpg/220px-Vajrasana%2C_early_20th_century.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2b/Vajrasana%2C_early_20th_century.jpg/330px-Vajrasana%2C_early_20th_century.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2b/Vajrasana%2C_early_20th_century.jpg/440px-Vajrasana%2C_early_20th_century.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Vajrasana,_early_20th_century.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The rediscovered <i><a href="/wiki/Vajrasana,_Bodh_Gaya" title="Vajrasana, Bodh Gaya">Vajrasana</a></i>, or "Enlightenment Throne of the Buddha", at the <a href="/wiki/Mahabodhi_Temple" title="Mahabodhi Temple">Mahabodhi Temple</a> in <a href="/wiki/Bodh_Gaya" title="Bodh Gaya">Bodh Gaya</a>. It was built by Ashoka to commemorate the enlightenment of the Buddha, about two hundred years before him.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAllen2012_122-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAllen2012-122">[121]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-123"><a href="#cite_note-123">[122]</a></sup></div></div></div>
<p>The <i>Mahavamsa</i> states that Ashoka ordered construction of 84,000 viharas (monasteries) rather than the stupas to house the relics.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEStrong1995158_124-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEStrong1995158-124">[123]</a></sup> Like <i>Ashokavadana</i>, the <i>Mahavamsa</i> describes Ashoka's collection of the relics, but does not mention this episode in the context of the construction activities.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEStrong1995158_124-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEStrong1995158-124">[123]</a></sup> It states that Ashoka decided to construct the 84,000 viharas when Moggaliputta Tissa told him that there were 84,000 sections of the Buddha's Dhamma.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEStrong1995159_125-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEStrong1995159-125">[124]</a></sup> Ashoka himself began the construction of the Ashokarama vihara, and ordered subordinate kings to build the other viharas. Ashokarama was completed by the miraculous power of Thera Indagutta, and the news about the completion of the 84,000 viharas arrived from various cities on the same day.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEStrong1995155_31-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEStrong1995155-31">[30]</a></sup>
</p><p>The construction of following stupas and viharas is credited to Ashoka:<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (November 2019)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>
</p>
<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Sanchi" title="Sanchi">Sanchi</a>, <a href="/wiki/Madhya_Pradesh" title="Madhya Pradesh">Madhya Pradesh</a>, India</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Dhamek_Stupa" title="Dhamek Stupa">Dhamek Stupa</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sarnath" title="Sarnath">Sarnath</a>, <a href="/wiki/Uttar_Pradesh" title="Uttar Pradesh">Uttar Pradesh</a>, India</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Mahabodhi_Temple" title="Mahabodhi Temple">Mahabodhi Temple</a>, <a href="/wiki/Bihar" title="Bihar">Bihar</a>, India</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Barabar_Caves" title="Barabar Caves">Barabar Caves</a>, <a href="/wiki/Bihar" title="Bihar">Bihar</a>, India</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Nalanda" title="Nalanda">Nalanda</a> Mahavihara (some portions like Sariputta Stupa), <a href="/wiki/Bihar" title="Bihar">Bihar</a>, India</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Taxila" title="Taxila">Taxila</a> University (some portions like <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Dharmarajika" title="Dharmarajika">Dharmarajika</a> Stupa and Kunala Stupa), <a href="/wiki/Taxila" title="Taxila">Taxila</a>, Pakistan</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Bhir_Mound" title="Bhir Mound">Bhir Mound</a> (<i>reconstructed</i>), <a href="/wiki/Taxila" title="Taxila">Taxila</a>, Pakistan</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Bharhut" title="Bharhut">Bharhut</a> <a href="/wiki/Stupa" title="Stupa">stupa</a>, <a href="/wiki/Madhya_Pradesh" title="Madhya Pradesh">Madhya Pradesh</a>, India</li>
<li><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Deorkothar" title="Deorkothar">Deorkothar</a> Stupa, <a href="/wiki/Madhya_Pradesh" title="Madhya Pradesh">Madhya Pradesh</a>, India</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Butkara_Stupa" title="Butkara Stupa">Butkara Stupa</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Swat,_Pakistan" title="Swat, Pakistan">Swat, Pakistan</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Sannati" title="Sannati">Sannati</a> Stupa, <a href="/wiki/Karnataka" title="Karnataka">Karnataka</a>, India</li>
<li>Mir Rukun Stupa, <a href="/wiki/Nawabshah" title="Nawabshah">Nawabshah</a>, Pakistan</li></ul>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Propagation_of_Dhamma">Propagation of Dhamma</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ashoka&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Propagation of Dhamma">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Ashoka's rock edicts suggest that during his eighth–ninth regnal years, he made a pilgrimage to the Bodhi Tree, started propagating dhamma, and performed social welfare activities. The welfare activities included establishment of medical treatment facilities for humans and animals; plantation of medicinal herbs; and digging of wells and plantation of trees along the roads. These activities were conducted in the neighbouring kingdoms, including those of the Cholas, the Pandyas, the Satiyaputras, Tamraparni, the Greek kingdom of Antiyoka.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGuruge,_Unresolved199550_126-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGuruge,_Unresolved199550-126">[125]</a></sup>
</p><p>The edicts also state that during his tenth–eleventh regnal years, Ashoka became closer to the Buddhist sangha, and went on a tour of the empire that lasted for at least 256 days.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGuruge,_Unresolved199550_126-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGuruge,_Unresolved199550-126">[125]</a></sup>
</p><p>By his 12th regnal year, Ashoka had started inscribing edicts to propagate dhamma, having ordered his officers (<i>rajjukas</i> and <i>pradesikas</i>) to tour their jurisdictions every five years for inspection and for preaching <i>dhamma</i>. By the next year, he had set up the post of the <i>dharma-mahamatra</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGuruge,_Unresolved199550_126-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGuruge,_Unresolved199550-126">[125]</a></sup>
</p><p>During his 14th regnal year, he commissioned the enlargement of the stupa of Buddha Kanakamuni.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGuruge,_Unresolved199550_126-3"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGuruge,_Unresolved199550-126">[125]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Third_Buddhist_Council">Third Buddhist Council</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ashoka&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Third Buddhist Council">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Third_Buddhist_council" title="Third Buddhist council">Third Buddhist council</a></div>
<p>The Sri Lankan tradition presents a greater role for Ashoka in the Buddhist community.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEStrong1995143_28-4"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEStrong1995143-28">[27]</a></sup> In this tradition, Ashoka starts feeding monks on a large scale. His lavish patronage to the state patronage leads to many fake monks joining the sangha. The true Buddhist monks refuse to co-operate with these fake monks, and therefore, no <a href="/wiki/Uposatha" title="Uposatha">uposatha</a> ceremony is held for seven years. The king attempts to eradicate the fake monks, but during this attempt, an over-zealous minister ends up killing some real monks. The king then invites the elder monk <a href="/wiki/Moggaliputta-Tissa" title="Moggaliputta-Tissa">Moggaliputta-Tissa</a>, to help him expel non-Buddhists from the monastery founded by him at Pataliputra.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGombrich19957_113-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGombrich19957-113">[112]</a></sup> 60,000 monks (<a href="/wiki/Bhikkhu" title="Bhikkhu">bhikkhus</a>) convicted of being heretical are de-frocked in the ensuing process.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEStrong1995143_28-5"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEStrong1995143-28">[27]</a></sup> The uposatha ceremony is then held, and Tissa subsequently organises the <a href="/wiki/Third_Buddhist_council" title="Third Buddhist council">Third Buddhist council</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGombrich19958_127-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGombrich19958-127">[126]</a></sup> during the 17th regnal year of Ashoka.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGuruge,_Unresolved199551_128-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGuruge,_Unresolved199551-128">[127]</a></sup> Tissa compiles <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Kathavatthu" title="Kathavatthu">Kathavatthu</a></i>, a text that reaffirms Theravadin orthodoxy on several points.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGombrich19958_127-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGombrich19958-127">[126]</a></sup>
</p><p>The North Indian tradition makes no mention of these events, which has led to doubts about the historicity of the Third Buddihst council.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEStrong1995144_29-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEStrong1995144-29">[28]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Nava_Jetavana_Temple_-_Shravasti_-_014_King_Asoka_at_the_Third_Council_(9241725897).jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1811" data-file-width="3021" decoding="async" height="132" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/33/Nava_Jetavana_Temple_-_Shravasti_-_014_King_Asoka_at_the_Third_Council_%289241725897%29.jpg/220px-Nava_Jetavana_Temple_-_Shravasti_-_014_King_Asoka_at_the_Third_Council_%289241725897%29.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/33/Nava_Jetavana_Temple_-_Shravasti_-_014_King_Asoka_at_the_Third_Council_%289241725897%29.jpg/330px-Nava_Jetavana_Temple_-_Shravasti_-_014_King_Asoka_at_the_Third_Council_%289241725897%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/33/Nava_Jetavana_Temple_-_Shravasti_-_014_King_Asoka_at_the_Third_Council_%289241725897%29.jpg/440px-Nava_Jetavana_Temple_-_Shravasti_-_014_King_Asoka_at_the_Third_Council_%289241725897%29.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Nava_Jetavana_Temple_-_Shravasti_-_014_King_Asoka_at_the_Third_Council_(9241725897).jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Ashoka and Monk <a href="/wiki/Moggaliputta-Tissa" title="Moggaliputta-Tissa">Moggaliputta-Tissa</a> at the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Third_Buddhist_Council" title="Third Buddhist Council">Third Buddhist Council</a>. Nava Jetavana, <a href="/wiki/Shravasti" title="Shravasti">Shravasti</a>.</div></div></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Richard_Gombrich" title="Richard Gombrich">Richard Gombrich</a> argues that the non-corroboration of this story by inscriptional evidence cannot be used to dismiss it as completely unhistorical, as several of Ashoka's inscriptions may have been lost.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGombrich19958_127-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGombrich19958-127">[126]</a></sup> Gombrich also argues that Asohka's inscriptions prove that he was interested in maintaining the "unanimity and purity" of the Sangha.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGombrich19958–9_129-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGombrich19958–9-129">[128]</a></sup> For example, in his Minor Rock Edict 3, Ashoka recommends the members of the Sangha to study certain texts (most of which remain unidentified). Similarly, in an inscription found at Sanchi, Sarnath, and Kosam, Ashoka mandates that the dissident members of the sangha should be expelled, and expresses his desire to the Sangha remain united and flourish.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGombrich19955_130-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGombrich19955-130">[129]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGuruge,_Unresolved199545_131-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGuruge,_Unresolved199545-131">[130]</a></sup>
</p><p>The 8th century Buddhist pilgrim <a href="/wiki/Yijing_(monk)" title="Yijing (monk)">Yijing</a> records another story about Ashoka's involvement in the Buddhist sangha. According to this story, the earlier king <a href="/wiki/Bimbisara" title="Bimbisara">Bimbisara</a>, who was a contemporary of the Gautama Buddha, once saw 18 fragments of a cloth and a stick in a dream. The Buddha interpreted the dream to mean that his philosophy would be divided into 18 schools after his death, and predicted that a king called Ashoka would unite these schools over a hundred years later.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEThapar196126_85-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEThapar196126-85">[84]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Buddhist_missions">Buddhist missions</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ashoka&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Buddhist missions">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>In the Sri Lankan tradition, <a href="/wiki/Moggaliputta-Tissa" title="Moggaliputta-Tissa">Moggaliputta-Tissa</a> – who is patronised by Ashoka – sends out nine Buddhist missions to spread Buddhism in the "border areas" in <abbr title="circa">c.</abbr><span style="white-space:nowrap;"> 250 BCE</span>. This tradition does not credit Ashoka directly with sending these missions. Each mission comprises five monks, and is headed by an elder.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGombrich199510_132-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGombrich199510-132">[131]</a></sup> To Sri Lanka, he sent his own son <a href="/wiki/Mahinda_(Buddhist_monk)" title="Mahinda (Buddhist monk)">Mahinda</a>, accompanied by four other Theras – Itthiya, Uttiya, Sambala and Bhaddasala.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEStrong1995143_28-6"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEStrong1995143-28">[27]</a></sup> Next, with Moggaliputta-Tissa's help, Ashoka sent Buddhist missionaries to distant regions such as Kashmir, Gandhara, Himalayas, the land of the Yonas (Greeks), Maharashtra, Suvannabhumi, and Sri Lanka.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEStrong1995143_28-7"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEStrong1995143-28">[27]</a></sup>
</p><p>The Sri Lankan tradition dates these missions to Ashoka's 18th regnal year, naming the following missionaries:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGuruge,_Unresolved199550_126-4"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGuruge,_Unresolved199550-126">[125]</a></sup>
</p>
<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Mahinda_(Buddhist_monk)" title="Mahinda (Buddhist monk)">Mahinda</a> to Sri Lanka</li>
<li>Majjhantika to Kashmir and Gandhara</li>
<li>Mahadeva to Mahisa-mandala (possibly modern Mysore region)</li>
<li>Rakkhita to Vanavasa</li>
<li>Dhammarakkhita the Greek to Aparantaka (western India)</li>
<li>Maha-dhamma-rakkhita to Maharashtra</li>
<li>Maharakkhita to the Greek country</li>
<li>Majjhima to the Himalayas</li>
<li>Soṇa and Uttara to Suvaṇṇabhūmi (possibly Lower Burma and Thailand)</li></ul>
<p>The tradition adds that during his 19th regnal year, Ashoka's daughter <a href="/wiki/Sanghamitta" title="Sanghamitta">Sanghamitta</a> went to Sri Lanka to establish an order of nuns, taking a sapling of the sacred Bodhi Tree with her.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGombrich199510_132-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGombrich199510-132">[131]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGuruge,_Unresolved199551_128-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGuruge,_Unresolved199551-128">[127]</a></sup>
</p><p>The North Indian tradition makes no mention of these events.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEStrong1995144_29-3"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEStrong1995144-29">[28]</a></sup> Ashoka's own inscriptions also appear to omit any mention of these events, recording only one of his activities during this period: in his 19th regnal year, he donated the Khalatika Cave to ascetics to provide them a shelter during the rainy season. Ashoka's <a href="/wiki/Minor_Pillar_Edicts" title="Minor Pillar Edicts">Pillar Edicts</a> suggest that during the next year, he made pilgrimage to Lumbini – the place of Buddha's birth, and to the stupa of the Buddha Kanakamuni.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGuruge,_Unresolved199551_128-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGuruge,_Unresolved199551-128">[127]</a></sup>
</p><p>The Rock Edict XIII states that Ashoka's won a "dhamma victory" by sending messengers to five kings and several other kingdoms. Whether these missions correspond to the Buddhist missions recorded in the Buddhist chronicles is debated.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGombrich19956_133-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGombrich19956-133">[132]</a></sup> Indologist <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Etienne_Lamotte" title="Etienne Lamotte">Etienne Lamotte</a> argues that the "dhamma" missionaries mentioned in Ashoka's inscriptions were probably not Buddhist monks, as this "dhamma" was not same as "Buddhism".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGombrich199510–11_134-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGombrich199510–11-134">[133]</a></sup> Moreover, the lists of destinations of the missions and the dates of the missions mentioned in the inscriptions do not tally the ones mentioned in the Buddhist legends.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGombrich199511_135-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGombrich199511-135">[134]</a></sup>
</p><p>Other scholars, such as <a href="/wiki/Erich_Frauwallner" title="Erich Frauwallner">Erich Frauwallner</a> and <a href="/wiki/Richard_Gombrich" title="Richard Gombrich">Richard Gombrich</a>, believe that the missions mentioned in the Sri Lankan tradition are historical.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGombrich199511_135-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGombrich199511-135">[134]</a></sup> According to these scholars, a part of this story is corroborated by archaeological evidence: the <i>Vinaya Nidana</i> mentions names of five monks, who are said to have gone to the Himalayan region; three of these names have been found inscribed on relic caskets found at <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bhilsa" title="Bhilsa">Bhilsa</a> (near <a href="/wiki/Vidisha" title="Vidisha">Vidisha</a>). These caskets have been dated to the early 2nd century BCE, and the inscription states that the monks are of the Himalayan school.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGombrich199510_132-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGombrich199510-132">[131]</a></sup> The missions may have set out from Vidisha in central India, as the caskets were discovered there, and as Mahinda is said to have stayed there for a month before setting out for Sri Lanka.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGombrich199511–12_136-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGombrich199511–12-136">[135]</a></sup>
</p><p>According to Gombrich, the mission may have included representatives of other religions, and thus, Lamotte's objection about "dhamma" is not valid. The Buddhist chroniclers may have decided not to mention these non-Buddhists, so as not to sideline Buddhism.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGombrich199512_137-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGombrich199512-137">[136]</a></sup> Frauwallner and Gombrich also believe that Ashoka was directly responsible for the missions, since only a resourceful ruler could have sponsored such activities. The Sri Lankan chronicles, which belong to the Theravada school, exaggerate the role of the Theravadin monk Moggaliputta-Tissa in order to glorify their sect.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGombrich199512_137-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGombrich199512-137">[136]</a></sup>
</p><p>Some historians argue that Buddhism became a major religion because of Ashoka's royal patronage.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEThapar199532_138-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEThapar199532-138">[137]</a></sup> However, epigraphic evidence suggests that the spread of Buddhism in north-western India and Deccan region was less because of Ashoka's missions, and more because of merchants, traders, landowners and the artisan guilds who supported Buddhist establishments.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEThapar199536_139-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEThapar199536-139">[138]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Violence_after_conversion">Violence after conversion</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ashoka&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Violence after conversion">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>According to the <i>Ashokavadana</i>, Ashoka resorted to violence even after converting to Buddhism. For example:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEStrong1995149_140-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEStrong1995149-140">[139]</a></sup>
</p>
<ul><li>He slowly tortured Chandagirika to death in the "hell" prison.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEStrong1995149_140-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEStrong1995149-140">[139]</a></sup></li>
<li>He ordered a massacre of 18,000 heretics for a misdeed of one.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEStrong1995149_140-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEStrong1995149-140">[139]</a></sup></li>
<li>He launched a pogrom against the Jains, announcing a bounty on the head of any heretic; this resulted in the beheading of his own brother – Vitashoka.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEStrong1995149_140-3"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEStrong1995149-140">[139]</a></sup></li></ul>
<p>According to the <i>Ashokavadana</i>, a non-Buddhist in <a href="/wiki/Pundravardhana" title="Pundravardhana">Pundravardhana</a> drew a picture showing the Buddha bowing at the feet of the Nirgrantha leader Jnatiputra. The term nirgrantha ("free from bonds") was originally used for a pre-Jaina ascetic order, but later came to be used for Jaina monks.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEThapar196128_141-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEThapar196128-141">[140]</a></sup> "Jnatiputra" is identified with <a href="/wiki/Mahavira" title="Mahavira">Mahavira</a>, 24th Tirthankara of <a href="/wiki/Jainism" title="Jainism">Jainism</a>. The legend states that on complaint from a Buddhist devotee, Ashoka issued an order to arrest the non-Buddhist artist, and subsequently, another order to kill all the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ajivika" title="Ajivika">Ajivikas</a> in Pundravardhana. Around 18,000 followers of the Ajivika sect were executed as a result of this order.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEStrong1989232_142-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEStrong1989232-142">[141]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Beni2010_143-0"><a href="#cite_note-Beni2010-143">[142]</a></sup> Sometime later, another Nirgrantha follower in <a href="/wiki/Pataliputra" title="Pataliputra">Pataliputra</a> drew a similar picture. Ashoka burnt him and his entire family alive in their house.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Beni2010_143-1"><a href="#cite_note-Beni2010-143">[142]</a></sup> He also announced an award of one <a href="/wiki/Kushan_coinage" title="Kushan coinage">dinara</a> (gold coin) to anyone who brought him the head of a Nirgrantha heretic. According to <i>Ashokavadana</i>, as a result of this order, his own brother was mistaken for a heretic and killed by a cowherd.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEStrong1989232_142-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEStrong1989232-142">[141]</a></sup> Ashoka realised his mistake, and withdrew the order.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEThapar196128_141-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEThapar196128-141">[140]</a></sup>
</p><p>For several reasons, scholars say, these stories of persecutions of rival sects by Ashoka appear to be clear fabrications arising out of sectarian propaganda.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Beni2010_143-2"><a href="#cite_note-Beni2010-143">[142]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Steven2010_144-0"><a href="#cite_note-Steven2010-144">[143]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-LePhuoc2010_145-0"><a href="#cite_note-LePhuoc2010-145">[144]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Last_years">Last years</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ashoka&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Last years">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Tissarakkha_as_the_queen">Tissarakkha as the queen</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ashoka&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: Tissarakkha as the queen">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Ashoka's last dated inscription - the <a href="/wiki/Major_Pillar_Edicts#Major_Pillar_Edict_4" title="Major Pillar Edicts">Pillar Edict 4</a> is from his 26th regnal year.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGuruge,_Unresolved199551_128-3"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGuruge,_Unresolved199551-128">[127]</a></sup> The only source of information about Ashoka's later years are the Buddhist legends. The Sri Lankan tradition states that Ashoka's queen Asandhamitta died during his 29th regnal year, and in his 32nd regnal year, his wife <a href="/wiki/Tishyaraksha" title="Tishyaraksha">Tissarakkha</a> was given the title of queen.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGuruge,_Unresolved199551_128-4"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGuruge,_Unresolved199551-128">[127]</a></sup>
</p><p>Both <i>Mahavamsa</i> and <i>Ashokavadana</i> state that Ashoka extended favours and attention to the <a href="/wiki/Bodhi_Tree" title="Bodhi Tree">Bodhi Tree</a>, and a jealous Tissarakkha mistook "Bodhi" to be a mistress of Ashoka. She then used black magic to make the tree wither.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEStrong1995152_146-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEStrong1995152-146">[145]</a></sup> According to the <i>Ashokavadana</i>, she hired a sorceress to do the job, and when Ashoka explained that "Bodhi" was the name of a tree, she had the sorceress heal the tree.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEStrong1995152–153_147-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEStrong1995152–153-147">[146]</a></sup> According to the <i>Mahavamsa</i>, she completely destroyed the tree,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEStrong1995153_148-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEStrong1995153-148">[147]</a></sup> during Ashoka's 34th regnal year.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGuruge,_Unresolved199551_128-5"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGuruge,_Unresolved199551-128">[127]</a></sup>
</p><p>The <i>Ashokavadana</i> states that Tissarakkha (called "Tishyarakshita" here) made sexual advances towards Ashoka's son Kunala, but Kunala rejected her. Subsequently, Ashoka granted Tissarakkha kingship for seven days, and during this period, she tortured and blinded Kunala.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEStrong1995151_149-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEStrong1995151-149">[148]</a></sup> Ashoka then threatened to "tear out her eyes, rip open her body with sharp rakes, impale her alive on a spit, cut off her nose with a saw, cut out her tongue with a razor." Kunala regained his eyesight miraculously, and pleaded for mercy for the queen, but Ashoka had her executed anyway.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEStrong1995152_146-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEStrong1995152-146">[145]</a></sup> Kshemendra's <i>Avadana-kalpa-lata</i> also narrates this legend, but seeks to improve Ashoka's image by stating that he forgave the queen after Kunala regained his eyesight.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEStrong1995165_150-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEStrong1995165-150">[149]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Death">Death</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ashoka&amp;action=edit&amp;section=22" title="Edit section: Death">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>According to the Sri Lankan tradition, Ashoka died during his 37th regnal year,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGuruge,_Unresolved199551_128-6"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGuruge,_Unresolved199551-128">[127]</a></sup> which suggests that he died around 232 BCE.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKosmin201436_151-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKosmin201436-151">[150]</a></sup>
</p><p>According to the <i>Ashokavadana</i>, the emperor fell severely ill during his last days. He started using state funds to make donations to the Buddhist sangha, prompting his ministers to deny him access to the state treasury. Ashoka then started donating his personal possessions, but was similarly restricted from doing so. On his deathbed, his only possession was the half of a <a href="/wiki/Phyllanthus_emblica" title="Phyllanthus emblica">myrobalan fruit</a>, which he offered to the sangha as his final donation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEStrong198918_152-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEStrong198918-152">[151]</a></sup> Such legends encourage generous donations to the <i>sangha</i> and highlight the role of the kingship in supporting the Buddhist faith.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESingh2008332_57-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESingh2008332-57">[56]</a></sup>
</p><p>Legend states that during his cremation, his body burned for seven days and nights.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-153"><a href="#cite_note-153">[152]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Family">Family</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ashoka&amp;action=edit&amp;section=23" title="Edit section: Family">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Sanchi_King_Ashoka_with_his_Queens,_South_Gate,_Stupa_no._1.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2331" data-file-width="3215" decoding="async" height="218" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b4/Sanchi_King_Ashoka_with_his_Queens%2C_South_Gate%2C_Stupa_no._1.jpg/300px-Sanchi_King_Ashoka_with_his_Queens%2C_South_Gate%2C_Stupa_no._1.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b4/Sanchi_King_Ashoka_with_his_Queens%2C_South_Gate%2C_Stupa_no._1.jpg/450px-Sanchi_King_Ashoka_with_his_Queens%2C_South_Gate%2C_Stupa_no._1.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b4/Sanchi_King_Ashoka_with_his_Queens%2C_South_Gate%2C_Stupa_no._1.jpg/600px-Sanchi_King_Ashoka_with_his_Queens%2C_South_Gate%2C_Stupa_no._1.jpg 2x" width="300"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Sanchi_King_Ashoka_with_his_Queens,_South_Gate,_Stupa_no._1.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A king - most probably Ashoka - with his two queens and three attendants, in a relief at <a href="/wiki/Sanchi" title="Sanchi">Sanchi</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESingh2017162_2-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESingh2017162-2">[2]</a></sup> The king's identification with Ashoka is suggested by a similar relief at <a href="/wiki/Kanaganahalli" title="Kanaganahalli">Kanaganahalli</a>, which bears his name.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESingh2008333_154-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESingh2008333-154">[153]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESingh2017162_2-3"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESingh2017162-2">[2]</a></sup></div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Kanaganahalli_Asoka_with_inscription.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2996" data-file-width="1871" decoding="async" height="272" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6a/Kanaganahalli_Asoka_with_inscription.jpg/170px-Kanaganahalli_Asoka_with_inscription.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6a/Kanaganahalli_Asoka_with_inscription.jpg/255px-Kanaganahalli_Asoka_with_inscription.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6a/Kanaganahalli_Asoka_with_inscription.jpg/340px-Kanaganahalli_Asoka_with_inscription.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Kanaganahalli_Asoka_with_inscription.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Ashoka with his queen, at <a href="/wiki/Kanaganahalli" title="Kanaganahalli">Kanaganahalli</a> near <a href="/wiki/Sannati" title="Sannati">Sannati</a>, 1st–3rd century CE. The relief bears the inscription "Rāya Asoko" (𑀭𑀸𑀬 𑀅𑀲𑁄𑀓𑁄, "King Ashoka") in <a href="/wiki/Brahmi_script" title="Brahmi script">Brahmi script</a>. It depicts the king with his queen, two attendants bearing fly-whisks, and one attendant bearing an umbrella.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESingh2008333_154-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESingh2008333-154">[153]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESingh2017162_2-4"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESingh2017162-2">[2]</a></sup></div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Emperor_Ashoka_and_his_Queen_at_the_Deer_Park.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1096" data-file-width="670" decoding="async" height="360" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5f/Emperor_Ashoka_and_his_Queen_at_the_Deer_Park.jpg/220px-Emperor_Ashoka_and_his_Queen_at_the_Deer_Park.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5f/Emperor_Ashoka_and_his_Queen_at_the_Deer_Park.jpg/330px-Emperor_Ashoka_and_his_Queen_at_the_Deer_Park.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5f/Emperor_Ashoka_and_his_Queen_at_the_Deer_Park.jpg/440px-Emperor_Ashoka_and_his_Queen_at_the_Deer_Park.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Emperor_Ashoka_and_his_Queen_at_the_Deer_Park.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Emperor Ashoka and his Queen at the <a href="/wiki/Sarnath" title="Sarnath">Deer Park</a>. <a href="/wiki/Sanchi" title="Sanchi">Sanchi</a> relief.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESingh2017162_2-5"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESingh2017162-2">[2]</a></sup></div></div></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Queens">Queens</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ashoka&amp;action=edit&amp;section=24" title="Edit section: Queens">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Various sources mention five consorts of Ashoka: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Devi_(Ashoka%27s_wife)" title="Devi (Ashoka's wife)">Devi</a> (or Vedisa-Mahadevi-Shakyakumari), <a href="/wiki/Karuvaki" title="Karuvaki">Karuvaki</a>, Asandhimitra (Pali: Asandhimitta), <a href="/wiki/Padmavati_(wife_of_Ashoka)" title="Padmavati (wife of Ashoka)">Padmavati</a>, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tishyarakshita" title="Tishyarakshita">Tishyarakshita</a> (Pali: Tissarakkha).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMookerji19629_155-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMookerji19629-155">[154]</a></sup>
</p><p>Kaurvaki is the only queen of Ashoka known from his own inscriptions: she is mentioned in an edict inscribed on a pillar at Allahabad. The inscription names her as the mother of prince Tivara, and orders the royal officers (mahamattas) to record her religious and charitable donations.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEThapar196130_89-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEThapar196130-89">[88]</a></sup>  According to one theory, Tishyarakshita was the regnal name of Kaurvaki.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEThapar196130_89-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEThapar196130-89">[88]</a></sup>
</p><p>According to the <i>Mahavamsa</i>, Ashoka's chief queen was Asandhimitta, who died four years before him.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEThapar196130_89-3"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEThapar196130-89">[88]</a></sup> It states that she was born as Ashoka's queen because in a previous life, she directed a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pratyekabuddha" title="Pratyekabuddha">pratyekabuddha</a> to a honey merchant (who was later reborn as Ashoka).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEStrong1995146–147_156-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEStrong1995146–147-156">[155]</a></sup> Some later texts also state that she additionally gave the pratyekabuddha a piece of cloth made by her.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEStrong1995166_157-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEStrong1995166-157">[156]</a></sup> These texts include the <i>Dasavatthuppakarana</i>, the so-called Cambodian or Extended <i>Mahavamsa</i> (possibly from 9th–10th centuries), and the <i>Trai Bhumi Katha</i> (15th century).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEStrong1995166_157-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEStrong1995166-157">[156]</a></sup> These texts narrate another story: one day, Ashoka mocked Asandhamitta was enjoying a tasty piece of sugarcane without having earned it through her karma. Asandhamitta replied that all her enjoyments resulted from merit resulting from her own karma. Ashoka then challenged her to prove this by procuring 60,000 robes as an offering for monks.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEStrong1995166_157-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEStrong1995166-157">[156]</a></sup> At night, the guardian gods informed her about her past gift to the pratyekabuddha, and next day, she was able to miraculously procure the 60,000 robes. An impressed Ashoka makes her his favourite queen, and even offers to make her a sovereign ruler. Asandhamitta refuses the offer, but still invokes the jealousy of Ashoka's 16,000 other women. Ashoka proves her superiority by having 16,000 identical cakes baked with his royal seal hidden in only one of them. Each wife is asked to choose a cake, and only Asandhamitta gets the one with the royal seal.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEStrong1995167_158-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEStrong1995167-158">[157]</a></sup> The <i>Trai Bhumi Katha</i> claims that it was Asandhamitta who encouraged her husband to become a Buddhist, and to construct 84,000 stupas and 84,000 viharas.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEStrong1995167–168_159-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEStrong1995167–168-159">[158]</a></sup>
</p><p>According to <i>Mahavamsa</i>, after Asandhamitta's death, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tissarakkha" title="Tissarakkha">Tissarakkha</a> became the chief queen.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEThapar196130_89-4"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEThapar196130-89">[88]</a></sup> The Ashokavadana does not mention Asandhamitta at all, but does mention Tissarakkha as Tishyarakshita.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEStrong1995151_149-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEStrong1995151-149">[148]</a></sup> The <i>Divyavadana</i> mentions another queen called Padmavati, who was the mother of the crown-prince Kunala.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEThapar196130_89-5"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEThapar196130-89">[88]</a></sup>
</p><p>As mentioned above, according to the Sri Lankan tradition, Ashoka fell in love with Devi (or Vidisha-Mahadevi), as a prince in central India.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEThapar196122–23_75-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEThapar196122–23-75">[74]</a></sup> After Ashoka's ascension to the throne, Devi chose to remain at Vidisha than move to the royal capital Pataliputra. According to the <i>Mahavmsa</i>, Ashoka's chief queen was Asandhamitta, not Devi: the text does not talk of any connection between the two women, so it is unlikely that Asandhamitta was another name for Devi.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEThapar196123_160-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEThapar196123-160">[159]</a></sup> The Sri Lankan tradition uses the word <i>samvasa</i> to describe the relationship between Ashoka and Devi, which modern scholars variously interpret as sexual relations outside marriage, or co-habitation as a married couple.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELahiri201597-98_161-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELahiri201597-98-161">[160]</a></sup> Those who argue that Ashoka did not marry Devi argue that their theory is corroborated by the fact that Devi did not become Ashoka's chief queen in Pataliputra after his ascension.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELahiri201598_73-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELahiri201598-73">[72]</a></sup> The <i>Dipavamsa</i> refers to two children of Ashoka and Devi – Mahinda and <a href="/wiki/Sanghamitta" title="Sanghamitta">Sanghamitta</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEThapar196122_162-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEThapar196122-162">[161]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Sons">Sons</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ashoka&amp;action=edit&amp;section=25" title="Edit section: Sons">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Tivara, the son of Ashoka and Karuvaki, is the only of Ashoka's sons to be mentioned by name in the inscriptions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEThapar196130_89-6"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEThapar196130-89">[88]</a></sup>
</p><p>According to North Indian tradition, Ashoka had a son named Kunala.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEStrong1995144_29-4"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEStrong1995144-29">[28]</a></sup> Kunala had a son named Samprati.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEThapar196130_89-7"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEThapar196130-89">[88]</a></sup>
</p><p>The Sri Lankan tradition mentions a son called <a href="/wiki/Mahinda_(Buddhist_monk)" title="Mahinda (Buddhist monk)">Mahinda</a>, who was sent to Sri Lanka as a Buddhist missionary; this son is not mentioned at all in the North Indian tradition.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEStrong1995143_28-8"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEStrong1995143-28">[27]</a></sup> The Chinese pilgrim <a href="/wiki/Xuanzang" title="Xuanzang">Xuanzang</a> states that Mahinda was Ashoka's younger brother (Vitashoka or Vigatashoka) rather than his illgetimate son.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEThapar196124_163-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEThapar196124-163">[162]</a></sup>
</p><p>The <i>Divyavadana</i> mentions the crown-prince Kunala alias Dharmavivardhana, who was a son of queen Padmavati. According to Faxian, Dharmavivardhana was appointed as the governor of Gandhara.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEThapar196130_89-8"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEThapar196130-89">[88]</a></sup>
</p><p>The <i>Rajatarangini</i> mentions Jalauka as a son of Ashoka.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEThapar196130_89-9"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEThapar196130-89">[88]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Daughters">Daughters</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ashoka&amp;action=edit&amp;section=26" title="Edit section: Daughters">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>According to Sri Lankan tradition, Ashoka had a daughter named Sanghamitta, who became a Buddhist nun.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGuruge,_Unresolved199549_117-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGuruge,_Unresolved199549-117">[116]</a></sup> A section of historians, such as <a href="/wiki/Romila_Thapar" title="Romila Thapar">Romila Thapar</a>, doubt the historicity of Sanghamitta, based on the following points:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEThapar196123–24_164-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEThapar196123–24-164">[163]</a></sup>
</p>
<ul><li>The name "Sanghamitta", which literally means the friend of the Buddhist Order (sangha), is unusual, and the story of her going to Ceylon so that the Ceylonese queen could be ordained appears to be an exaggeration.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEThapar196123_160-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEThapar196123-160">[159]</a></sup></li>
<li>The <i>Mahavamsa</i> states that she married Ashoka's nephew Agnibrahma, and the couple had a son named Sumana. The contemporary laws regarding exogamy would have forbidden such a marriage between first cousins.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEThapar196124_163-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEThapar196124-163">[162]</a></sup></li>
<li>According to the <i>Mahavamsa</i>, she was 18 years old when she was ordained as a nun.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEThapar196123_160-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEThapar196123-160">[159]</a></sup> The narrative suggests that she was married two years earlier, and that her husband as well as her child were ordained. It is unlikely that she would have been allowed to become a nun with such a young child.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEThapar196124_163-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEThapar196124-163">[162]</a></sup></li></ul>
<p>Another source mentions that Ashoka had a daughter named Charumati, who married a kshatriya named Devapala.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEThapar196130_89-10"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEThapar196130-89">[88]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Brothers">Brothers</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ashoka&amp;action=edit&amp;section=27" title="Edit section: Brothers">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>According to the <i>Ashokavadana</i>, Ashoka had an elder half-brother named Susima.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEStrong1989204_50-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEStrong1989204-50">[49]</a></sup> According to the Sri Lankan tradition, Ashoka killed his 99 half-brothers.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGombrich19957_113-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGombrich19957-113">[112]</a></sup>
</p><p>Various sources mention that one of Ashoka's brothers survived his ascension, and narrate stories about his role in the Buddhist community.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEThapar196127–28_165-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEThapar196127–28-165">[164]</a></sup>
</p>
<ul><li>According to Sri Lankan tradition, this brother was Tissa, who initially lived a luxurious life, without worrying about the world. To teach him a lesson, Ashoka put him on the throne for a few days, then accused him of being an usurper, and sentenced him to die after seven days. During these seven days, Tissa realised that the Buddhist monks gave up pleasure because they were aware of the eventual death. He then left the palace, and became an <i>arhat</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEThapar196127_86-3"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEThapar196127-86">[85]</a></sup></li>
<li>The Theragatha commentary calls this brother Vitashoka. According to this legend, one day, Vitashoka saw a grey hair on his head, and realised that he had become old. He then retired to a monastery, and became an arhat.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEThapar196128_141-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEThapar196128-141">[140]</a></sup></li>
<li>Faxian calls the younger brother Mahendra, and states that Ashoka shamed him for his immoral behaviour. The brother than retired to a dark cave, where he meditated, and became an arhat. Ashoka invited him to return to the family, but he preferred to live alone on a hill. So, Ashoka had a hill built for him within Pataliputra.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEThapar196128_141-3"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEThapar196128-141">[140]</a></sup></li>
<li>The Ashoka-vadana states that Ashoka's brother was mistaken for a Nirgrantha, and killed during a massacre of the Nirgranthas ordered by Ashoka.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEThapar196128_141-4"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEThapar196128-141">[140]</a></sup></li></ul>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Imperial_extent">Imperial extent</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ashoka&amp;action=edit&amp;section=28" title="Edit section: Imperial extent">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1096954695/mw-parser-output/.tmulti" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="thumb tmulti tright"><div class="thumbinner multiimageinner" style="width:392px;max-width:392px"><div class="trow"><div class="tsingle" style="width:194px;max-width:194px"><div class="thumbimage" style="height:179px;overflow:hidden"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Maurya_Empire,_c.250_BCE_2.png"><img alt="" data-file-height="1121" data-file-width="1200" decoding="async" height="179" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2c/Maurya_Empire%2C_c.250_BCE_2.png/192px-Maurya_Empire%2C_c.250_BCE_2.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2c/Maurya_Empire%2C_c.250_BCE_2.png/288px-Maurya_Empire%2C_c.250_BCE_2.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2c/Maurya_Empire%2C_c.250_BCE_2.png/384px-Maurya_Empire%2C_c.250_BCE_2.png 2x" width="192"/></a></div><div class="thumbcaption">Ashoka's empire stretched from <a href="/wiki/Afghanistan" title="Afghanistan">Afghanistan</a> to <a href="/wiki/Bengal" title="Bengal">Bengal</a> to southern India. Several modern maps depict it as covering nearly all of the Indian subcontinent, except the southern tip.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-HKDR_2004_map_166-0"><a href="#cite_note-HKDR_2004_map-166">[165]</a></sup></div></div><div class="tsingle" style="width:194px;max-width:194px"><div class="thumbimage" style="height:179px;overflow:hidden"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Maurya_Empire,_c.250_BCE.png"><img alt="" data-file-height="1121" data-file-width="1200" decoding="async" height="179" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c4/Maurya_Empire%2C_c.250_BCE.png/192px-Maurya_Empire%2C_c.250_BCE.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c4/Maurya_Empire%2C_c.250_BCE.png/288px-Maurya_Empire%2C_c.250_BCE.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c4/Maurya_Empire%2C_c.250_BCE.png/384px-Maurya_Empire%2C_c.250_BCE.png 2x" width="192"/></a></div><div class="thumbcaption"><a href="/wiki/Hermann_Kulke" title="Hermann Kulke">Hermann Kulke</a> and <a href="/wiki/Dietmar_Rothermund" title="Dietmar Rothermund">Dietmar Rothermund</a> believe that Ashoka's empire did not include large parts of India, which were controlled by autonomous tribes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-HKDR_2004_map_166-1"><a href="#cite_note-HKDR_2004_map-166">[165]</a></sup></div></div></div></div></div>
<p>The extent of the territory controlled by Ashoka's predecessors is not certain, but it is possible that the empire of his grandfather Chandragupta extended across northern India from the western coast (Arabian Sea) to the eastern coast (Bay of Bengal), covering nearly two-thirds of the <a href="/wiki/Indian_subcontinent" title="Indian subcontinent">Indian subcontinent</a>. Bindusara and Ashoka seem to have extended the empire southwards.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGombrich19951_167-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGombrich19951-167">[166]</a></sup> The distribution of Ashoka's inscriptions suggests that his empire included almost the entire <a href="/wiki/Indian_subcontinent" title="Indian subcontinent">Indian subcontinent</a>, except its southernmost parts. The Rock Edicts 2 and 13 suggest that these southernmost parts were controlled by the Cholas, the Pandyas, the Keralaputras, and the Satiyaputras. In the north-west, Ashoka's kingdom extended up to <a href="/wiki/Kandahar" title="Kandahar">Kandahar</a>, to the east of the <a href="/wiki/Seleucid_Empire" title="Seleucid Empire">Seleucid Empire</a> ruled by <a href="/wiki/Antiochus_II_Theos" title="Antiochus II Theos">Antiochus II</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESingh2017162_2-6"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESingh2017162-2">[2]</a></sup> The capital of Ashoka's empire was <a href="/wiki/Pataliputra" title="Pataliputra">Pataliputra</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Magadha" title="Magadha">Magadha</a> region.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGombrich19951_167-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGombrich19951-167">[166]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Religion_and_philosophy">Religion and philosophy</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ashoka&amp;action=edit&amp;section=29" title="Edit section: Religion and philosophy">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Relationship_with_Buddhism">Relationship with Buddhism</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ashoka&amp;action=edit&amp;section=30" title="Edit section: Relationship with Buddhism">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Upasaka.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="328" data-file-width="816" decoding="async" height="88" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/ca/Upasaka.jpg/220px-Upasaka.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/ca/Upasaka.jpg/330px-Upasaka.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/ca/Upasaka.jpg/440px-Upasaka.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Upasaka.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The word <i><a href="/wiki/Up%C4%81saka" title="Upāsaka">Upāsaka</a></i> (𑀉𑀧𑀸𑀲𑀓, "Buddhist lay follower", in the <a href="/wiki/Brahmi_script" title="Brahmi script">Brahmi script</a>), used by Ashoka in his <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Minor_Rock_Edict" title="Minor Rock Edict">Minor Rock Edict</a> No.1 to describe his affiliation to Buddhism (circa 258 BCE).</div></div></div>
<p>The Buddhist legends state that Ashoka converted to Buddhism,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEStrong198915_168-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEStrong198915-168">[167]</a></sup> although this has been debated by a section of scholars.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEStrong1995142_169-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEStrong1995142-169">[168]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Minor_Rock_Edicts#Full_texts_of_the_Minor_Rock_Edicts" title="Minor Rock Edicts">Minor Rock Edict 1</a> leaves no doubt that Ashoka was a follower of Buddhism. In this edict, he calls himself an <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Up%C4%81saka_and_Up%C4%81sik%C4%81" title="Upāsaka and Upāsikā">upasaka</a> (a lay follower of Buddhism) and a <i>sakya</i> (i.e. Buddhist, after Gautama Buddha's title <i>Shakya-Muni</i>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELahiri2015134_170-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELahiri2015134-170">[169]</a></sup> This and several other edicts are evidence of his Buddhist affiliation:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGuruge,_Unresolved199543–44_171-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGuruge,_Unresolved199543–44-171">[170]</a></sup>
</p>
<ul><li>In his Minor Rock Edict 1, Ashoka adds that he did not make much progress for a year after becoming an <i>upasaka</i>, but then, he "went to" the Sangha, and made more progress. It is not certain what "going to" the Sangha means – the Buddhist tradition that he lived with monks may be an exaggeration, but it clearly means that Ashoka was drawn closer to Buddhism.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGombrich19953_172-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGombrich19953-172">[171]</a></sup></li>
<li>In his Minor Rock Edict 3, he calls himself an <i>upasaka</i>, and records his faith in the Buddha and the Sangha.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEThapar199519–20_173-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEThapar199519–20-173">[172]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGuruge,_Unresolved199544_174-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGuruge,_Unresolved199544-174">[173]</a></sup></li>
<li>In the <a href="/wiki/Major_Rock_Edicts#Major_Rock_Edict_8" title="Major Rock Edicts">Major Rock Edict 8</a>, he records his visit to Sambodhi (the sacred Bodhi Tree at Bodh Gaya), ten years after his coronation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGuruge,_Unresolved199544_174-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGuruge,_Unresolved199544-174">[173]</a></sup></li>
<li>In the <a href="/wiki/Lumbini#Pillar_of_Ashoka" title="Lumbini">Lumbini (Rumminidei) inscription</a>, he records his visit to the Buddha's birthplace, and declares his reverence for the Buddha and the sangha.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEStrong198913_91-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEStrong198913-91">[90]</a></sup></li>
<li>In the Nigalisagar inscription, he records his doubling in size of a stupa dedicated to a former Buddha, and his visit to the site for worship.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGombrich19955_130-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGombrich19955-130">[129]</a></sup></li>
<li>Some of his inscriptions reflect his interest in maintaining the Buddhist sangha (see <a href="#Purification_of_sangha">#Purification of sangha</a> below).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGombrich19955_130-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGombrich19955-130">[129]</a></sup></li>
<li>The <a href="/wiki/Saru_Maru" title="Saru Maru">Saru Maru</a> inscription states that Ashoka dispatched the message while travelling to Upunita-vihara in Manema-desha. Although the identity of the destination is not certain, it was obviously a Buddhist monastery (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Vihara" title="Vihara">vihara</a>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELahiri2015157_175-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELahiri2015157-175">[174]</a></sup></li></ul>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Other_religions">Other religions</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ashoka&amp;action=edit&amp;section=31" title="Edit section: Other religions">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>A legend in the Buddhist text <i>Vamsatthapakasini</i> states that an Ajivika ascetic invited to interpret a dream of Ashoka's mother had predicted that he would patronise Buddhism and destroy 96 heretical sects.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEThapar196126_85-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEThapar196126-85">[84]</a></sup> However, such assertions are directly contradicted by Ashoka's own inscriptions. Ashoka's edicts, such as the Rock Edicts 6, 7, and 12, emphasise tolerance of all sects.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEThapar199529_176-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEThapar199529-176">[175]</a></sup> Similarly, in his <a href="/wiki/Major_Rock_Edicts#Major_Rock_Edict_12" title="Major Rock Edicts">Rock Edict 12</a>, Ashoka honours people of all faiths.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEStrong19894_177-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEStrong19894-177">[176]</a></sup> In his inscriptions, Ashoka dedicates caves to non-Buddhist ascetics, and repeatedly states that both Brahmins and shramanas deserved respect. He also tells people "not to denigrate other sects, but to inform themselves about them".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGombrich19953_172-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGombrich19953-172">[171]</a></sup>
